THE RED PAGE.

It. is hard in Australia to got even a nod-
ding acquaintance with recent foreign literature
other than British, for the books never reach us.
Is there a single Australian who could pass an
examination in Huysman, Maeterlinck, or Ver-
haeren ? —to say nothing of Verlaine or Baudelaire,
or others comparatively far-back. Yet Maeter-
linck seems probably quite as notable as Tolstoi,
and Verhaeren and Verlaine have far more poetical
significance than the rabbits of the English warren,
like Kipling or Watson, of whom every local body
with a smattering of letters can appraise the
merits. And what, generally speaking, do we
know of Russian literature, beyond the well-ad ver-
tised names—or of German, or of Scandinavian ?
Really, if somebody would establish a cosmopoli-
tan bookshop, and keep the Southern Cross
abreast of the northern lights, it would be a Boon.
But it would need cosmopolitan capacity to con-
duct the bookshop—and, if it paid at all, it
would pay only little and slowly. There is much
more profit in selling Anthony Hope and Marie
Corelli to the undiscriminating female.
* * * »
A note on Emile Verhaeren in Nov. Fort-
nightly brings a little knowledge of that very
strong and skilful poet, one of the leaders of a
minor Belgian renascence. (Scant comfort to
find that he is almost unknown in England.)
Born near Antwerp in 1855, he studied law as a
youth, and as a young man left law for literature.
His chief field was L’Art Moderne , a Brussels
periodical, to which he contributed critical prose
and melancholy verse. He has written much—
and almost all his work is distinguished and
impressive. The vers libre is his preferred medium
—moulded and colored to his thought—with lines
now long, now short, rhymed or unrhymed, linked
in verses or otherwise, as the spirit wills. An ex-
ample of his color-verse in the manner of Verlaine
is given—
La neige tombe indiscontintiment
Comme une lente et longue et pauvre laine
Parmi la morne et longue et pauvre plaine
Froide d'amour, chaude de haine.
• * • *
Some weeks ago these exquisite lines by Ver-
haeren were printed, with a suggestion that trans-
lations should be sent :
PIEUSEMENT.
La nuit d’hiver 61£ve au ciel son pur calice.
Et je live mon coeur aussi, mon coeur nocturne,
Seigneur, mon coeur! vers ton p&le infini vide,
Et n&mmoins, je sais que rien n’en pourra l’urne
Combler, et que rien n’est dont ce coeur meurt avide,
Et je te sais mensonge et mes Ifevres te prient
Et roes genoux ; je sais et tes grandes mains closes
Et tes grands yeux ferrous aux ddsespoirs qui orient,
Et que c’est moi, qui, seul, roe rfeve dans ies choses;
Sois de pitid, Seigneur I pour ma toute ddiuence,
J’ai besoin de pleurer mon mal vers ton silence. . . .
La nuit d’hiver (‘■l&ve au ciel son pur calice.
If the versions received represent local capacity,
the gentle art of translating is still to acquire in
Australia. Only one of fair merit came to hand,
from T.S.R. ; there were tolerable from M.N.,
A. 8., Adam, and Patroclus ; and worthless from
G.M.V.K., .T.G.D., H.A.J., 8.F., H.M.J.,
M.F.K., Anon, Mt. Barker, J.J., and R.F.L.
* * * *
Some of the translators did not know the mean-
ing of the French ; some were not in tune with
Verhaeren’s spirit; some were too lazy to hunt for
the just word, and sent approximate paraphrases ;
some padded intolerably, and altered the poem’s
swing and sense together— e.g. :
The winter night lifts up to heaven
Its chalice pure—its cup—
Its snowy cup—the wintry night
To the still skies lifts up.
Which is meant to represent Verhaeren’s single
line
La nuit d’hiver elfeve au ciel son pur calice.
As an example of another kind —
The winter night hei chalice lifts on high.
And I uplift my winter heart of night,
O God ! towards thy pale Infinity.
Ana yet I know ’tis but an empty height
And ’tis for Nothing that I thirst and die.
I know thou art a Lie. . . .
Line 1: pur is omitted, and the sense of the
flower-freshness of the night goes with it. Worse
is the indefinite rendering of au ciel by “on
high.” Almost the best of the poem’s magic
comes in the imaged correspondence between
the uplifting of the Night and that of the
Soul towards something : and this vanishes
unless the toivards sense is got in. To make the
vanishment complete, aussi is omitted from line 2.
The force of the treble mon cceur is slurred ; the
one word vide is expanded to a line; line 4, to
combler in line 5, is omitted altogether, and the
remainder of line 5 is mistranslated. And so on,
with the final result of misinterpreting Verhaeren
with discreditable dexterity. For a translator is
no translator if he omit a single nuance of the
author’s thought, or insert a single nuance of
thought which is not the author’s. That to begin
with ; and when the spirit is accurately reflected,
form and melody and atmosphere must be copied
also till there is no room for the acutest caviller
to get the point of his nib in.
* * » *
Here is T.S.R.’s version—
ASPIRATION.
The winter Night her chalice lifts to Heaven.
And I too lift my heart, my night-tuned heart,
O Lord, my heart! to thy pale, endless Void,
Though knowing well its cup can by no art
Be filled, and naught it craves when death-destroyed:
A lie I know thee, whom my lips beseech,
To whom I bow : thy folded hands I see,
Thy great eyes closed ’gainst all despairing speech.
’Tis I who dream myself into infinity.
On all my madness. Lord ! O shed thy halm:
I needs must weep my trouble to thy calm. . . .
The winter Night her chalice lifts to Heaven.
“ Aspiration,” for the title, is good. English
“ pious” is shallow compared with French pieux :
it does not bring the same sense of veneration and
solemnity: “ piety,” for us, has become rather a
ridiculous, bourgeois emotion, linked with the
spectacle of a fat grocer kneeling with a fat
prayer-book to consider not the lilies, how they
grow, but the customers, how they pay. Pur is
again omitted—a loss ; but something is gained by
personifying Night, and the correspondence of
the toivards is preserved. Lines 2 ;ind 3 seem
about as good as they can be got, and if there is a
better word for nocturne than “ night-tuned,” in
the circumstances, one wants to know what.
Lines 4 and 5 weaken a little, but not much, and,
considering their difficulty, the quatrain is a thing
to have modified pride in. T.S.R. gives an alter-
native line 4 :
Knowing its thirsty cup can by no art—
and defends “ thirsty ” as developing Verhaeren’s
thought as he would have done himself had
rhyme and rhythm permitted. “The best of
poets feels to some extent the trammels of rhyme.
Suppose that V. wrote equally well in French and
English : it is certain that in each language he
would find words and sounds which would express
different phases of his theme better than any
words and sounds in the other. Now, is it not
permitted to a translator of the French Verhaeren
to vary the mould of thought as V. might have
varied it had he written in English? In other
words, where his French obviously cramps V.,
is it better to cramp the English to match? or to
expand the idea, since English permits it? I
sa y_ eX pand. For example, the word urne has no
special virtue. It is only there because it sounds
well, and rhymes with nocturne. But it is awk-
wardly metonymyous for cceur. Tasse or coupe
would really match calice much better, but tasse
hasn’t the dull, luxurious roll of urne , and it doesn’t
Iyme. Plainly yon must translate uine-n t_by
e English “ urn ” vou can’t fancy the h art
lifting its urn to Heaven—hut by cup, wn c
habitually uplifted by the flowers suggested m
lice. And if you add ‘ thirsty,’you strengthen
e idea of heart-drouth which inspires the poem,
Pile perpetuating the flower-illusion >
iwer holds its thirsty cup for dew—and dew
is a language-association with parched hear
* * • *
This doctrine looks very much like that which
■fends evil-doing for the sake of the good which
ay come, and one would not, like to trust it 1
e hands of any lesser translator than-bw n-
irne, say. Reverting to T.S.R. :it is plain th
s does not close so well as he began : his ideas are
it identical with Yerhaeren’s, and Mere are
her flaws— e.g. , “despairing speech IS .
r “ desespoirs qui orient.” But on the whole he
is succeeded fairlv, and with another fortnight
lent in unstiffening it his version might be good..

Walk in ! walk in ! to G. W. Steevens’ cele-
irated classical waxworks, every figure as large
is life, with a concealed phonograph under its
vaisteoat or petticoat as the case may be. Ho !
lere is Caligula ! Tune up, Caligula ! . . .
Still there, you ? Who are you? Have you my fiat to
)6 alive ? Stop 1 Did I tell you who lam ? They call me
Caligula, but I am not Caligula. They say I’m Gaius
Tubus Augustus, the grandson of that cad Agrippa, but
t’m not Gaius Julius Augustus. And I’m not by Agrippa,
I’m not, indeed. They say I’m Mars or Jupiter, hut I m
not Mars or Jupiter. And do you know who lam truly ?
[ami! I! Do you understand? That is all—l. lam
ord of the world ; lam all things. I smell blood ; I shed
t. I see clouds ; I blew them out of a bubble-blower,
genus is waiting for me; she is dying of love for me. I
jhall clutch her and bathe in the bottomless beds of her
;yes, and sink my teeth in her flesh. She will scream
vith anguish when I embrace her. I shall tear her
lelicious body to cutlets and fillets, that none shall come
ifter me. I do not want a daughter to perpetuate me.
[am perpetual: perpetual am I! I shall pile up all the
rold of the world and swallow it. I shall cut the throats
if all the world, men and women and babies, and drink
the blood. Then I shall wax and swell till I burst through
heaven and squash the stars like flies on the walls of
ipace. Then I shall shove down outwards, and extend
on and on, for ever and ever and ever. There will exist
nothing, nothing at. only I. Great, perfect, only, all
I! Oh! 1.. . I . I. . .
* * * *
There! is not that a better Caligula than you will
mould from many pages of history ? Probably he
did not really look in the least like that : the
most consummate modern showman can only
build on the basis of the extraordinary traits that
astonished C.’s contemporaries ; but for a con-
ventional Caligula this is as good as you will get.
And here is Xanthippe! Kindly turn the
handle. . .
Well, that evening I loved him. I remember it as well
as if it had been yesterday. I had stewed a kid for
supper with garlic ; you get the relish of it better, if you
do it that way. Well, as we were having supper in the
court of the house—it was summer ;if you leaned back
you could see the figs getting streaked against the blue
sky till your eyes got dazzled. “ Well,” I said, “Socrates,
you must eat some garlic or else I won’t.” He said
“Why?” He was always asking why. I said, “Be-
cause,” and then he laughed at me. That hurt me like.
I began to get afraid it wasn’t going off as well as I thought
it would. So I got up, arid put my arms round his great
hairy neck, and said, “Socrates, I love you.” Then he
laughed again, and said, “ Why do you love me, Xan-
thippe?” Then I got all hot and flustered, because he
laughed at me again. I could never hear being laughed
at by a man. But for all that I answered him quite

. . . J. D. Hennessey, the busy Sydney writer, is hurrying
down the literary hill as fast as his pen will carry him.
His first book, “ The Dishonorable,” showed something
of the stress and pang of thought—was clever and pro-
mising. His second, “ Wynnum,” was frank, easily-
written rubbish. His third, “An Australian Bush
Track,” is rather worse—the crudest imagination mixed
with bits of neat enervation and description which show
how well H. could write if he took pains. And it is
advertised as eomplaisantly greased by Govt. House.
Govt. House ought to know better.
FOR THE BULLETIN—
THE DEATH OF GOD.
God had a dream
And, waiting', with a smile
He gave it life and breath and earthly hue s
“ Go and redeem
Some soul, entombed in guile.”—
That dream was -Heaven incarnated—you t
And then we met,
And in your eyes I saw
The promise of a Paradise untrod ;
Half-blinded yet,
I recognised the Law,
The peerless image of a living God.
Ah, me! You fell,
And died in reckless shame—
Then Heaven and its angels vanished too :
Yours was their knell;
You bore their holy name ;
And God fell with you, and He died, like you.
S. VON KOTZE.

Bards of the Backbloeks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A LITTLE MISTAKE.
Tis a yarn I heard of a new-chum trap
wu *1 ed^e the Never-Never,
v here the dead men lie and the black men lie,
And the bushman lies for ever.
1 was the custom still with the local blacks
tvT° ™ the “altogether ”
a J lad ess respect for our feelings then,
And more respect for the weather.
The t r °oper said to the sergeant’s wife :
-p bure, I wouldn’t seem unpleasant;
a j ere s women and childer r.bout the place,
And barrin’ a lady’s present—
There s ould King Billy wid niver a stitch
-bor a month —may the drought cremate him !
wa - n we ' n his dhirty head,
Where his ould Queen Mary bate him.
God give her strength ! —and a peaceful reign—
i hough she flics in a bit av a passion
ony w an hints that her shtoyle an’ Inks
Are a trifle behind the fashion.
d here s two of the boys by the stable now—
Be the powers ! I’ll teach the varmints
lo come wid nought but a shirt apiece,
An wid dirt for their lower garmints.
‘ % owld on > ye blaggards ! How dare ye dare
1° come widin sight av the houses?
111 give ye a warnin’ all for wanco
An’ a couple of pair of trousers.”
They took the pants as a child a toy,
The constable’s words beguiling
A smile of something beside their joy ;
And they took their departure smiling.
And that very day, when the sun was low,
Two blackfellovvs came to the station ;
They were filled with the courage of Queensland
rum
And bursting with indignation.
The constable noticed, with growing ire,
They’d apparently dressed in a hurry ;
And their language that day, I am sorry to say,
Mostly consisted of “ plurry.”
The constable heard, and he wished himself back
f( In the land of the bogs and the ditches—
“ You plurry big tight-britches pleeceman, what
for
You gibbit our missuses britches ? ”
And this was a case, I am bound to confess,
Where civilisation went under ;
Had one of the gins been less modest in dress
He’d never have made such a blunder.
And here let the moral be duly made known,
And hereafter signed and attested :
We should place more reliance on that which is
shown
And less upon what is suggested.
HENRY LAWSON.
THE MEN WHO WENT BEFORE.
(An answer to Lawson's tale of the Men who Come
Behind.)
And it doesn’t strike you, Lawson, that “the
men who come behind ’’
Have sufficient cause for sorrow —and you
needn’t make it more—
Humping journalistic bluey where a thousand
tracks are lined,
Everything that’s worth the having snapped by
“ men who went befoi’e.”
You inay try to save the country, spend your life
in politics,
And you’ll find some cause or other leaves you
sadly in the wet,
Whilst you ask for cash to run it all the Press gets
heaving bricks—
“ Men who went before ” could borrow —you’ve
just got to pay their debt.
Go and seek a free selection ; nothing’s left but
rock and scrub,
All the soil that God created, all the dust on
earth is gone ;
Friendly owners warn you mildly with the meek
and lowly club
That they’re men who came beforehand, and
you’re forced to battle on.
Try your hand at mining, maybe, search for a
secluded spot
Where no other pick has ventured and the
shovel's quite unknown,
Spend a year in burrowing under till you think
the scent is hot
Thud ! the pick goes through and rattles on a
heap of human bone.
Tramp the tracks in search of tucker when it’s
useless cadging work
And your pride is getting streaky with the signs
of dead’ning rust —
You must tail that sad procession of adopted
“ Sons of Shirk ”
While the crowd ahead get tucker, you—come
in for all the dust.
Then you die and tramp to Heaven with the pass
you’ve earned below
And request the sleepy Peter to unlock the
jasper door,
But he tells you slowly, sadly, that you've got no
holy show,
For every inch available’s been taken up before.
Off to hell you go despairing—but the doors are
open wide,
And a bed and board eternal’s something
when your feet are sore.
Then you grasp the creed astounding as you gently
downward slide—-
“Men are brothers! Hell is roomy.” All the
rest have gone before.
Vic.
L.M.D.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 23, 1897.
“The Bulletin’s” Federal Constitution.
During the last few years this paper has
published many articles on different aspects
of Federation, both as to what the Constitu-
tion should be and what it should not be.
But it has never yet attempted to summarise
what it regards as an ideal Federal Constitu-
tion, nor, so far as it knows, has any other
paper done so. Considering that the Con-
vention will shortly be sitting, unless some
further mishap occurs to the machine
within the next few weeks, the present
seems as good a time as any to pre-
sent The Bulletin’s Federal Constitution.
It is by no means a complete affair,
space being limited, but it covers the
leading points of the case, and covers them
as well as this paper knows how to do it.
Also, it isn’t absolutely ideal ; The Bulletin
itself can make out a remarkably strong case
against several of its own proposals, but it
advances them simply because there is a
difficulty which must be faced, and it can
make out an even stronger case against any
other solution. Especially on financial
questions and questions of representation it
seems impossible to arrive at absolute justice
to all parties by any system of Federation,
and it is still more impossible to do so by
not federating at all, inasmuch as none of
the provincial systems provide any decently
impartial method of dividing the expenditure
among the people who contribute the re-
venue, or dividing the representation so as
to give microscopic justice to every person
and class and interest. Therefore, the fol-
lowing outline is presented as the best and
fairest compromise among many difficulties
that The Bulletin can fashion.
* » • *
(1) The Federation to be knoWn as the Australian Com-
monwealth or Dominion or United Provinces or simply as
Australia, whichever way a majority of the Convention
may decide. Any name, as long as it isn’t too long or
too uneuphonious.or isn’Aalready in use somewhere else,
will serve the purpose.
(2) The Legislature to consist of two Houses. This is
partly because the bi-cameral system is so habitual that
an attempt at the unicameral system would probably
break up the whole scheme. Also, partly because the
existence of a Second Chamber will leave room for a com-
promise which will prevent New South Wales and Victoria
absolutely dominating the Legislature, and thus scaring
away the smaller provinces through the fear that their
independence will be wholly sacrificed.
(3). The Lower House to consist of, say, 200 members
elected by manhood or manhood and womanhood suffrage
only, there being 200 constituencies returning one member
each. The constituencies to be divided out according to
population in the most convenient manner, with no re-
gard whatever for provincial boundaries. The number
200 is suggested not because so many are essential for law-
makingpurposes, but because with a much smaller number
there would be a danger of making the electorates so
large as to be unmanageable. A proportionate increase
to be made when Queensland or M.L. or both of them
join the Federation.
(4.) The Upper House to consist of, say, 10 or 15 mem-
bers from each province elected on the same suffrage as the
Lower House, but each province to vote as one con-
stituency. This will undoubtedly give undue weight to
the less populous provinces until the diffusion of popula-
tion equalises matters, which it will do to a great extent
by-and-bye. But, on the other hand, if both Houses are
elected on a population basis the bogey of a possible
combination between Victoria and N.S.W. to run the
Federal finances for their own sole benefit W'ould probably
prevent some of the w'eaker provinces joining at all. The
need to make some sacrifice in the cause of unity sticks
out conspicuously just here.
(5.) Both Houses to expire or dissolve and be re-elected
simultaneously.
(6.) Members of the Lower House to receive, say, £3OO
a year salary ; members of the Upper House, say, £4OO or
£5OO. The exact figure is a matter of small importance,
but the extra salary is suggested in the case of the Upper
House partly because elections will be more expensive in
that chamber of vast constituencies.
(7.) Ministers to be elected individually at the meeting
of the new Parliament by both Houses sitting and voting
together, provision being made, however, that neither
House must contain less than tw»o Ministers. Each,
Minister to hold office for the full term of Parliament
unless he previously dies, resigns, becomes incapacitated
by illness (the conditions which constitute incapacity to
be defined by Parliament), is convicted of crime, or is
removed by a majority vote in both Houses. Ministers to
be eligible for re-election to an unlimited extent.
(8.) The two Houses to have equal power in all matters.
All points of disagreement to be settled without discus-
sion by the vote of both Houses sitting as one.
(9.) Any matter to be referred to a Referendum vote if
a certain fixed number of the people or a certain fixed
proportion (say one-third) of the combined Federal Legis-
lature or a majority of tbe Provincial Legislatures so
request. The Referendum vote in ordinary cases to be
taken (for reasons of economy) at the general election
only. An absolute majority of both Houses of the
Federal Legislature, however, may direct a popular vote
to be taken at any time in order to meet oases of emer-
gency.
(10.) The figurehead above the Legislature to be a
Governor-General appointed as at present, because there
is no better method just now available. When the
Federation is complete the Parliament of Australia may
insist on electing its Governor-General or replacing him
by a President, but until that Parliament is constituted
there is no authority strong enough to do so. And as the
Federal Parliament will be indefinitely postponed if it
doesn’t start till the imported British figurehead is first
done away with, it will be necessary to put up with the
usual Carrington or Brassey for the present.
(11.) The Federal Legislature to take over and consoli-
date all the public debts of the several States without
regard to the fact that some are larger in proportion to
population or are better represented by assets than
others.
(12.) The Federal Government to take over, as its
principal sources of revenue, the railway, postal and
telegraph receipts, and customs and excise revenue, and
to formulate a uniform customs and excise tariff through-
out its territories, all internal customs tariffs being
abolished.
(13,) The Federal Government to own and meet all
expenses connected with the railways, postal and tele-
graph departments, the military and naval forces —all
military and naval stores being handed over along with
the command of the forces, mints, patent office, the
entire public education system, the Supreme Courts, and
a number of smaller matters, including all rivers which
flow through more than one province. Where the public
debts taken over have been partly incurred for the
purpose of making loans to local bodies, these debts to
become Federal property.
(14.) In ease the sources of revenue above described
should prove insufficient, the Federal Government to have
unlimited powers of taxation to meet the deficit. In case
they are more than sufficient, the surplus to be used for
public works or in reduction of the public debt. The dis-
crepancies which arise under this system will, at least, be
no worse than those that exist under the provincial
arrangement, and, in course of time, things will probably
equalise themselves as nearly as possible. Anyhow, there
is no more reason why the Federal surplus, if there is one,
should be handed back in mathematically accurate pro-
portions to the respective provinces than there is for
handing back the provincial surplus in exact proportions
to each town and hamlet, according to the amounts
received from them and expended on them. If the pre-
sent system is good enough for Australia provincially, it
is probably good enough for the same place federally.
(15.) The Provincial Parliaments to retain the land
revenues, proceeds of probate and succession duties, land,
income, and dividend taxes, business licenses, port and
harbor dues, fines, and to have the necessary powers of
taxation to make up any shortage. But no borrowing
powers whatever to remain with the Provincial Legisla-
tures. Borrowing is only justifiable, if justifiable at all,
when undertaken for works which yield direct revenue,
and, as practically all the revenue-producing undertakings
will be in the hands of the Federal Government, the only
purpose of any moment for which the provinces could
want to borrow is with a view to faking the accounts and
producing bogus surpluses.
(16.) The Provincial Parliaments to control irrigation
works, ports and harbors, roads, bridges, and other purely
local works, police, inferior courts, &c. But —
(17.) Their powers of law-making to be defined by the
Federal Legislature wherever necessary. Their constitu-
tions to be capable of amendment by the Federal Legisla-
ture, especially with a view to the fact that one Chamber
of very moderate size and an elective Governor at, say,
£lOOO a year, would be fully sufficient for the local pur-
poses of each province. All powers not expressly reserved
to them to belong to the Federal authority. The Federal
Legislature to have power to define and extend its own
limits and define and extend or diminish those of the
State Legislatures wherever necessary. This last is prac-
tically the power which the State Legislatures now
possess with regard to municipal legislatures, and it is
absolutely necessary that there should be such power some-
where if perpetual conflict is to be avoided. Either the
people of Australia in their united capacity must be em-
powered to decide, when occasion arises, what authority
over themselves they shall exercise through the Federal
authority, and what they shall exercise provincially, or
there will some day be a deadlock. In any case, it will be
the people governing themselves ; the essential is that
where the people in one capacity collide with themselves
in another capacity they must have some means of sort-
ing the matter out.
(18.) The Federal Constitution to contain no provision
whatever for secession. There is no such provision in
any of the provincial constitutions or in the constitution
of any civilised State that The Bulletin is aware of. This
doesn’t in itself prove anything, but to make provision
for secession is merely to invite secession.
(19.) The Federal Legislature to meet provisionally at
some place to be agreed upon, and decide on its own
capital. The constitution-makers would do well, how-
ever, to recommend that the Federal city shall not be
selected from among the existing provincial capitals.
(20). The Federal capital and its immediate locality to
bi a Federal district belonging to and under the control
of the Federal authority only.
(21.) The Federal Legislature to be specifically entrusted
with the formation of a uniform criminal code and uni-
form laws relating to quarantine, marriage, divorce,
patents and copyrights, and banking and companies
generally. This latter is essential, for a multitude of
banks and companies do business in a large number of
provinces, and no provincial authority can regulate more
than the small part of their business which is done within
its own territory.
(22.) The Federal authority to have the sole power of
issuing paper-money. It may exercise this power directly
by issuing State paper-money from the Treasury, or
through a State bank, or indirectly by regulating the
issue of paper-money by private banks. ‘ State banks or
land banks run by Provincial Governments to have no
note-issuing powers except such as may be delegated to
them by the Federal authority.
(23.) The Federal Constitution to be capable of amend
ment in any direction on the vote of an abserendum. 3 °' 3
of the Federal Legislature endorsed bj ltele
The above outline is necessarily very im-
perfect but it expresses as clearly as P os&l^ e
The Bulletin’s opinions on the P Ol V£ *
warding which most controversy has u*
arisen. The central idea is that the peop
should trust the Federal authority as com-
pletely as possible, or not at all. It win re-
present the whole people, and, so far as ca
be devised, it will be the people, and l
people can’t trust themselves—whom can they
trust ? It will attract all the biggest men in
Australian public life, leaving the second-rate
men or the men who have not yet made
their mark to run the provincial legislatures,
and when troubles arise, as they must m the
imperfect nature of things, between ie
central and the local authorities it is neces-
sary to decide whether the balance of power
shall lie with the best men or the merely
second-best. The Bulletin votes for letting
it lie with the former every time. It is im-
possible to mffke things balance quite
mathematically ; some day it may be
found that there is either too much power
with the Federal authority or too much with
the provincial ones, and this paper believes
in letting the Federal authority have the
benefit of the doubt. One tyrant is bettei
than six ; one fool is also better than six. It
is impossible to fix up any scheme by which
each province will contribute exactly the
same amount per head in Federal revenue
and receive exactly the same amount in
Federal expenditure ; and if it were possible
to do so now the scheme would be hopelessly
awry ten years hence, and utterly useless in
less than fifty years ; therefore, The Bulle-
tin proposes that the people should trust
themselves to do a reasonably fair thing by
themselves in their own interests, and not
seek out elaborate devices for tying them-
selves to something lest they should break
loose and do themselves serious injury
especially as there is nothing that they can
tie themself to which is large enough to hold
them. So long astheconstitutionprovidesthat
the Federal Legislature shall be the supreme
authority in Australia, that it shall be en-
tirely representative of the people, and that
it can be amended at any time by an easy
and accessible expression of the popular will,
the future will be as safe as any human in-
genuity can make it. These things being
provided, the Federal system will be just as
good an institution as the people of Aus-
tralia deserve it should be.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The “Herald’s” Apology.
The New South Wales Government is getting
on well with that new protective policy which
it and its press barrackers allege isn’t Protec-
tion but a specially-advanced variety of Foreign-
trade. It recently let a big bridge contract to
a New South Wales firm at a price considerably
above a Victorian tender, the only visible
reason being that it was right and profitable for
the State to support local industry, even at
some small immediate expense to the taxpayer.
This is the view which every Protectionist
takes, but it sounds like an ignoble and ridicu-
lous back-down when advanced by a Freetrade
Government which has denounced over and over
again the “ bolstering up ” of industries by
“ artificial means,” and declared that they
must stand entirely on their own merits in free
competition with the whole world, or else go
under. It has been further decided to have
the poles and brackets for the new Sydney elec-
tric trams made locally, regardless of possible
discrepancies in the price, and to shut out the
foreign competitor altogether. The Govern-
ment is also calling for tenders for a huge
quantity of blankets and for 150,000 tons of
iron rails, and in both cases it is stipulated that
the goods are to be manufactured in New South
Wales. It isn’t simply proposed to handicap
the foreign maker with a duty, which is partial
Protection, but to exclude him entirely, which
is the whole animal in its most aggravated form ;
yet, strangely enough, the two Sydney ultra-
Freetrade papers both support these extraordin-
ary proceedings, and allege that while it is Pro-
tection to partly shut out the foreigner with a
15 per cent, duty, to shut him out altogether is
Freetrade, pure and undefiled. Both the
Herald and Telegraph stated proudly in
their issue of Jan. 13 that the steel rails already
alluded to, if manufactured locally, would only
cost <£150,000, or from that up to £300,000,
more than if they were imported, and neither
journal said one word against “bolstering up
the steel-rail industry ” with the taxpayers’
money, or “ coddling the local manufacturer,”
or “creating an exotic industry which couldn’t
stand on its own merits,” or uttered any of the
other curses which were evoked when the Dibbs-
Lyne Government followed exactly the same
policy over the much smaller iron-pipe con-
tract. The Herald, in fact, publishes a special
apology in its leading columns which is an
interesting sample of the abject and crawlsome
art of explaining things away :
Even if the locally-made rails should be dearer than
imported, the result will not be so bad as in the case
quoted, for by the operation of that pipe contract the
entire community does not pay the increased cost, hut
the unfortunate town that has to take the pipes. But
the principle is the same. It has always been recognised
that the Government might properly encourage the local
manufacture of such articles as were needed in consider-
able quantities. This could be done by paying up to
about 15 per cent, above the price of the imported article
in consideration of the advantages of having the material
made under local supervision, of saving interest charges
upon the very large stocks otherwise carried, and, of
course, employing the people remuneratively. It causes
pain and surprise in the minds of Victorian I r -tectionists
to witness the attempts of the Government cf this colony
to stimulate local industry by giving local contractors the
preference instead of by the orthodox method, which is
to shut out th: importer altogether. These critics look
upon a blanket or a steel-rail contract as true Protection
masked by a hypocritical Freetrade. They miss the point
altogether, which is that, while the action of the Govern-
ment in taking a local tender at a margin over the im-
ported price is quite voluntary, Protection compels
Government and community to take the local tender at
any margin up to the amount of duty plus freights.
Besides, we are none of us so extreme in the practice of
our fiscal faith. , . ,
The last argument is about the most abject
piece of stupidity which even the boss stupid
paper ever "perpetrated. According to it, it
the people put a Protectionist Government in
office, and openly impose Protection on them-
selves by their own act, and deliberately decide
to support local industries for their own benefit
then the proceeding isn’t voluntary. And
when they put a Freetrade Government in
power with direct instructions not to protect
or subsidise any local industry whatever,
and that Government proceeds to su b«>-
dise the local steel-rail biz. at the cost of
the community to the extent of somewhere
between £150,000 and ,£300,000 on no authority
at all, it is quite voluntary. In other words, if
Protection is forced on the people without their
consent it is voluntary, and if they openly
adopt it then it isn’t voluntary. And the poor
mongrel Herald admits that “ the principle is
the same ” as regards the Dibbs pipe contract
and the proposed Reid steel rails contract, yet
it furiously cursed the former and mildly blesses
the latter ; and it said the former was Protec-
tion while the later isn’t. Also, it is quite
right for the Reid Government to “ encourage
local manufactures” by paying prices that are
15 per cent, higher than is necessary under the
blessed principle of Free Competition. Also,
“ we are none of us extreme in the practice of
our fiscal faith ” —not now, at all events. Our
colonial oath, no 1 Have the Fairfaxes any
fiscal faith left now to be extreme about ?

The Threatened New Guinea Kush.
Interesting to those who meditate prospecting
in New Guinea :
Inland, British New Guinea, 7th Nov., ’96.
Since my last to you we made a start for Mt. Victoria,
but have made poor progress, and are doubtful if we shall
be able to get through at all. Where we are camped now
is a native village, about 30 miles south of Mt. Victoria,
The people here carried us on for two daj's, and would
then go no further. Two stayed with one of our party
to guard the camp, myself and two others went on for
two days further to try and pick up fresh natives ; but
although we got within 15 miles of the Mount, we
could not see a single native or the slightest sign of track,
so there was nothing for it but to return to main camp
and start the two carriers back for the remainder and
return here. We are now going to try further to the
east, and may be successful in finding natives that way,
but without carriers we can do nothing, and if they can’t
be found there will be nothing for it but to return to
Port Moresby. We could get through ourselves easily
enough, but the loads we could carry would not keep us
for any time, therefore, even if on the field we would not
be able to do any good. The country is very broken and
the ranges very high (we crossed some 3000 ft.), and, worst
of all, very heavy scrub. All your track has to be cut, and
it is only at rare intervals you can see where you are going,
so there is no picking of road. You must just take them
as they come, and you can fancy what that means when the
hills are so high! They are generally also very steep, and
there is no level country to give you a spell. It’s up and
down all the way and you are also burdened with your
Winchester and revolver—these you must carry, and with
cartridges the weight tells up. A good fly is also an abso-
lute necessity, and quinine also —I had a touch of fever a
few days ago, but am again all right. This climate, how-
ever, is entirely different to the Australian and a man
cannot be too careful. Sickness here is no joke. I fancy
I mentioned in my last that the Governor returned and
also brought over 700 oz. gold. I saw some of it; its value
must be close to £4 per oz. The first thing he did on his
arrival in Port Moresby was to order that no hoys be
“ signed” till after the wet season, and especially men-
tioned our party. The law here is that you cannot take
a native more than 25 miles from where he is en-
gaged unless he is “signed” in a Govt, office. That
blocked us, and hence our present trouble. The
Gov. says that the south coast route is the only
practicable one by which to reach the field, and that
after the wet season is over the Gov. will cut a track.
Now if there is going to be a rush it will set in at once-
wet season or no wet season, and men will try and reach
the field in defiance of all opposition. The consequence
will be that fully 50 per cent, will leave their bones behind
them. If Govt, acted promptly with a wish to do good
and cut a road straight away it would be the saving of
many lives. So far since I have been out from Port
Moresby (about three weeks) the weather has been very
good—three thunderstorms, but only very light, and we
were under shelter at the time. The wettest season of
the year is about the same as Queensland —January and
February. We may expect thunderstorms until then.
But where the great drawback and danger will lie is the
question of carriers. Now, the natives here are not over-
fond of hard work—they are much the same as others in
this respect.—but a few certain articles that the whites
have got they are very desirous of having, and will work
by carrying until they get them ; after that they will do
nothing except at very odd times. This is my" opinion,
and, I fancy, the opinion that any sensible man would
take when he comes to review the circumstances. So
that what it really amounts to is : the first few parties
may get carriers without any great trouble; after then,
the darkies will refuse. People coming here ought to be
informed of this, for, even if they get through to the field
itself, they won’t have rations to live on while there.
Burns, Philp and Co. are, as I have already informed you,
trying to open a track for horses, but even were it opened
there are, so far, very few horses here, and if the scrub
continues over to the main range there will he no feed :
so that for some time to come things will be very black
for new arrivals. Will write you again first opportunity,
if able to get through, which may not be for some time.
13th. —We are again on the river that we turned back
from (the Goldie), but about 13 miles higher up. We
were very doubtful of getting the natives here to carry,
as they are at loggerheads with the Government, who
blame them for “wiping out” a village on the coast a
few months ago, and it is only by bringing some neigh-
bors of theirs, who are friends, t hat we have managed to
get hold of them. We leave in the morning, and, as this
tribe is a large one, they will be able to see us a long way
on our journey. This letter will be forwarded to the coast,
and it will be the last communication that we will have
with it till we reach the field.

Marks.
A PAPER is generally to be judged by the letters
it admits into its columns, and on this basis
the S.M. Herald is distinctly against Austra-
lian Federation. In its issue of January 14
the mild old paper came out with a perfect
rush of correspondence, and among other things
it introduced to its readers one Thomas Marks,
who deserves to be held in reverence as the
only humorous aspect of Federation up to date.
Marks complains among other things that “it
would be tlia bounden duty of a Federal Par-
liament to legislate in the interests of all Aus-
tralia, however injurious it might be to the
mother colony, and one of these duties would
be to divert a very great deal of N.S.W. pro-
duce to Melbourne and Adelaide as the nearest
and most convenient ports of shipment.” Marks
declares himself against Federation lot this
reason. The Federal Government, by abolish-
ing border tariffs and building its new mi ways
to point straight to the nearest port inst ad of
to the capital of the province, would gi\e many
N.S.W. producers in the south and west a
much more adjacent outlet, cheaper freights
shorter haulage, and would enable them to have
lines where now they can have none be ;ause
trade which will justify the building of a
200-mile line won’t justify a 500-mile one. And
Marks, if there is such a person, professes to


cheerfully damn Federation because it will open
the way to the “nearest and most conve-
nient pot t. Let us all barrack for the Sydney
importer and damn the nearest and most
convenient port Moreover, Marks suggests
that the Reid Government “can only treat
Freetrade at the ensuing conference as the
settled pohey of N.S.W. which, Federation or
no federation, it cannot, without a gross
breach of faith with those who twice returned
it to power, permit for one moment to be ques-
tioned This seems unnecessary violence on
the part of Marks. If the people of New South
Wales prefer Freetrade to Federation they can
say so by rejecting the Federal Constitution
when it is submitted to the national vote. If
the province wants Freetrade so badly as Marks
say it does it will say so; Marks’s idea that
because New South Wales has voted Freetrade
twice it shouldn t be trusted to vote again is
very steep. Marks also complains that a
k ederal Government couldn’t be trusted to make
Sydney “ the key-centre of the trade and com-
merce of all Australia,” which seems quite
probable—as likely as not, the Federal Govern-
ment will let the trade go where it likes, and
won t make any key-centre. Also, Marks says
that the Federal Government wouldn’t pay
the salary of the New South Wales irri-
gation expert, and that it isn’t dead
certain that it would make Sydney the
capital, and it might even refuse to irrigate
N.S.W. at the expense of all Australia, and
might tell that province to irrigate itself, at its
own expense, like the others. The Bulletin
is struggling with sundry conflicting theories
about the Herald’s correspondent. One idea
is that the Herald invented Marks in all good
faith and stupidity ; while another is that
Marks is a huge joke perpetrated on an unsus-
pecting newspaper, probably by John Haynes,
and that his letter is a hilarious burlesque on
the Herald itself. The third possibility is
that Marks made a bet with Samuels that the
Herald would be dull enough to take him
seriously, and is now collecting the stakes ; or
else he is an ardent Federationist who is sys-
tematically bringing the provincial crowd into
contempt. The last possibility of all seems to
be that Marks is a real individual, and that an
enemy put his name to the letter for the plea-
sure of hearing him foam in Yiddish when he
read the awful thing. Anyhow, it isn’t every
day that something so wildly amusing as Marks
dawns on a tired community, and it is to be
\ hoped he will write again. /
Pharmacy and Federation. /
W rites “ X.Z.” to The Bulletin :
“ Bumble ”-itis seems to have gripped the rulers of
N.S.W. pharmacy pretty badly. One Colin M. M’Creary
told the Pharmaceutical Society of N.S.W. that he had
taken up his residence in N.S.W., that he had served four
years’ apprenticeship with a chemist registered in N.S.W.
though not now keeping shop in N.S.W, and that he
wished to present himself for his final (qualificatory)
examination. In place of the N.S.W. preliminary certifi-
cate he held and showed the matriculation certificate of
Melbourne University, which has been accepted in
N.S.W. times out of number in place of the prelim-
inary certificate. About that there was naturally
no grumble. Councillor Marshall moved that M’Creary
be allowed to go up for his final on his Mel-
bourne certificate being approved, and promptly
stultified himself by stating that he did not wish the
admission of an apprentice from another province unless
that province first admitted N.S.W. apprentices, which
means—“ N.S.W. is willing to be reciprocal, provided
some other province is reciprocal first.” Which practi-
tically means, “ This hoard will give way if other Austra-
lasian boards will first lick its boots.” Seeing that N.S. W.
has about the crankiest pharmaceutic and medical law of
all the Australias, the position seems, to say the least
of it, illogical. And particularly so when Victoria
and Queensland accept N.S.W. certificates in a spirit
of compromise, whilst N.S.W., with an inferior en-
trance examination, refuses to accept the more
difficult North and South ones. Perhaps, after all, Coun-
cillor Marshall meant well (though probably M‘Crbary
won’t think so), for he moved—“ That this Board would
only be too glad to allow Mr. M’Creary to come up for
the final examination, but, while working under the pre-
sent law, the application cannot be granted ; this Board
recognises the hardship, but has no power to accede to
the request.” What is wanted is an Australasian phar-
maceutical standard, so that the staydard certificate of
one province will be accepted in any other. The same
with medicine. Melbourne University, for example
refuses to accept inferior records and certificates, but, at
the same time, will consider all such records and certifi-
cates, and gives recognition tot pro quot, which, inter-
preted, means “ Show me what you’ve done, and I’ll give
you credit for exactly what you’ve done.” In fact, both
in druggistry and medicine let Australia have a standard
Staats Examen or Government examination, on which
all licenses to practise should depend.
No Creeds in the Commonwealth.
“ Cardinal Moran, if elected as a delegate to
the Federal Convention, will consider it his
patriotic duty to sit.” People now begin to
guess the meaning of Cardinal Moran’s pre-
liminary canter at the mushroom “ convention ”
which sprang up so suddenly and unexpectedly
at Bathurst the other day. That so-called con-
vention, mainly sustained by the purse of one
of the Cardinal’s most dutiful servants, in the
light of the present seems, to a suspicious
public, to have been the Cardinal’s little
feeler : should a complaisant public make no
objection to him there, he could try a bigger
coup. Yet if Cardinal Moran, the official repre-
sentative of the R.C. Church in Australia,
sits as a member of the Commonwealth Con-
vention, the national aspect of that Convention
is gone at once, and the door is opened for a
renewal of the miserable feud of creeds. It may
be a matter of regret that a man of the Car-
dinal’s mental gifts should be barred from can-
didature, but facts must be faced : while the
tires re-lighted and fanned by O’Farrell and
Parkes still smoulder there can be no more
unfit candidate in the community than the local
head of any church—for The Bulletin makes
precisely the same objection to the admission of
the candidature of any other creed’s official re-
presentative —Anglican, Wesleyan, or what-
not. None of them can dissociate himself from
his clerical position, and Australia wants no
creeds in politics. In regard to the ordinary
candidate, The Bulletin does not enquire
whether he is Roman or Anglican, Mormon or
Devil-worshipper, it is all one ; but when the
professed exponent of one particular brand of re-
ligion—who is practically unknown apart from
his association with that religion —who can only
secure votes because he is the locally-recognised
head of that religion—comes forward in what
will be universally recognised as the sheep s
clothing of “ a simple citizen ” and asks to be
permitted to take part in the work of nation-
building—in which religious creeds can have no
more share than vipers—The Bulletin is sure
of the support of enlightened Australian Demo-
crats, of all men of unbiassed intelligence, in its
vehement national protest.
Co-Social-Operation.
Certain shallow optimistic ‘ thinkers ’ and
teachers have, of course, made the ludicrously
absurd mistake of confusing socialism with co-
operation, and imagining that opponents of one
must also be enemies of the other,” snorts the
deep-thinking Argus, as though the aforesaid
assumption were not derived from hard facts.
Anti-socialists, with their eternal cry for
“ private enterprise ” and “ individualism,”
have always scowled at co-operation, though
they are among the most ardent customers of
co-operative stores when they can save money
by going there. A system which deprives
Capital of its power to oppress Labor can never
be advocated by the “ respectables ” of this
world, who hate the word Socialism as the
devil is fictitiously supposed to hate holy water.
Suppose for a moment that Victoria had no
railways and Melbourne were without its sinful
Met. Gas Co. Would the Argus be in favor of
a wise creation of State railways, and a supply-
ing of gas by the municipality for the benefit
of co-operating shareholders and the public
at large? Not much. We don’t find the
bloated capitalist and his beloved news-
paper agitating for a municipal electric-lighting
company to shatter the gas monopoly, or sing-
ing praises to State ownership of land. Now,
State ownership of land is the socialist’s bright-
est dream of co-operation, and private enter-
prisers rail against the great principle accord-
ingly.
The “Pure Merino” University.
Thus “ Fax ” to The Bulletin :
The late J. H. Ciiallis gave a-quarter of a million ster-
ling for the advancement of education and science per
medium of Sydney University. Much of the interest of
this is sopped up by a coterie of mediocre Scotch medicos,
who, nevertheless, are admirably adapted to do the
drudgery of coaching up youths whose parents, not Nature,
have destined them to be sawbones. The professors of
history, engineering, and English, by drawing their
screws, assist also to carry out the beneficent intention of
the late testator. It didn’t strike the University Senate
that ML Challis didn’t specially desire to find billets for
commonplace professors, but seemed to want to assist
tmifliant youths to secure admission to professions for
.which their gifts adapted them. Now, here’s where the
r specious liberality of the Senate conies in : there is not a
single bursary ( i.e ., annual reward-subsidy to poor but
brilliant candidates) given in the medical, engineering,
science, or law school— i.e., in any of the schools whose
existence is due to the magnificent gifts of the
deceased merchant, but they are given with the greatest
lavishness to those seeking to secure that wretched gew-
gaw, the B.A. degree. Can it be that the Senate fear that
the professions would be encroached upon by “ a very
undesirable class ” ? . . . That the course of the medical
school is as unsatisfactory as might be anticipated
from the calibre of the public teachers in that
faculty was prominently illustrated a short time since.
Noble old Macleay, seeing the tremendous possibilities of
the Science of Bacteriology and the immense part it was
bound to play in the near future in the Theory and
Practice of Medicine, gave a substantial legacy to en-
courage the study of that subject, making one condition
—that a few terms study of that subject should be incor-
porated in the five years’ medical course. The Faculty
were in doubt for a long time if they should accept a
legacy with so onerous a condition ; and ultimately, after
some lawyers had a dig at. the fund, the Court of Equity,
with owl-like gravity, gave the Faculty permission to
carry on the old man’s scheme in an emasculated form.
The poor Senate had hardly got out of this dilemma when a
grievance from an unexpected quarter loomed up. Some
wild-eyed youths from out Grabben Gullen way and the
plains of Boomoonoomona want to know why can’t they
get this bauble of a B.A. degree without attending lec-
tures at the central institution? They ask, and not with-
out reason, that, as the University is largely supported
by general State revenue, they should get the alleged
“ Hall-Mark ” of a University degree, if without lectures
they can pass the same examinations as the men who
have the “ tremendous advantage that coming into con-
tact with one another gives.” They seem to think
they could learn off as glibly as the Sydney-coddled
variety literary criticisms of works that neither has ever
read, and they even presume to think that the possession
of some elementary, if well-known, text-books on history
and philosophy and standard keys of languages and
mathematics would be quite as useful as the dictated
lectures of the Sydney professors. Two other topics
should be referred to : the parsimony of the senate in in-
sufficiently manning the supervisory examinations com-
mittees has led to more than one grave scandal ; and
secondly, influence, not brains, determines who shall fill
vacancies in the teaching body. The truth of this latter
was demonstrated the other day. A lecturer was ap-
pointed in the Arts faculty, although he was beaten by at
least one gold medallist, and although several other
medallists about the town (belonging to the “ educated
proletariat”) would have jumped at the offer. To sum
up, the University is at present run in the interests of the
professors and the pure merinos. To remedy this (1) a
large number of bursaries and scholarships should be
established in the professional schools ; (2) the B.A.
degree should be given to any one passing the examina-
tions ; (3) a degree which would be a guarantee of some
degree of culture and scholarship should be conferred on
men who should be compelled to reside at the University.
This would probably require the establishment of an
additional non-sectarian college similar to the Women’s
College. (4) Fellowships for enthusiastic men likely to
do good scientific work after having obtained degrees
should be endowed. (5) The present teaching-staff should
be remunerated at their strict market-value ; and (6) the
good old Equity Court should be invoked to allow the
bequests of Challis, Fisher, Macleay, and others to be
devoted towards purposes somewhat resembling the in-
tention of the testators.
And thus “ Sancho Panza ” :
Dear Bulletin,— Since the Sydney University authori-
ties insist upon attendance at lectures, one would expect
the fees to be as reasonable as possible. But what is the
case ? In Melbourne (where attendance is not compul-
sory) the lecture-fees for the B.A. course are 12 guineas
per year—total for the three years, 36 guineas. In Sydney
the fees are :
For the first year ... ... ... £32 18 6
For second ~ ... ... ••• 28 4 0
~ third „ ... ••• ••• 21 18 0
Total for the three years, £B3 os. 6d .—i.e., more than
double the amount paid in Melbourne ! And yet
Sydney University is far more richly endowed than
that of Melbourne. But it may be urged that high fees
are paid in Sydney in order to secure the best teaching.
Well, here is my experience : The professor of a certain
subject which is compulsory for all first-year students
began his course of instruction by talking over our heads,
usfno- a lot of highly technical language which was abso-
lutely unintelligible to us—a fact which he himself well
knew. After three or four lectures the learned professor
informed us, in his own peculiar sneering way, that
he was not there to teach us, and from that on to the
end of the course he fully demonstrated the truth
of this statement. None of us learned anything from his
lectures. Our time would have been more profitably em-
ployed in playing cricket, or doing the Block. As for the
f ees __but that is too sore a point to dwell upon. Another
member of the teaching staff always had his lecture
written out, and read it through in a monotonous, weari-
some style, just as a 10-year-old boy might be expected to
do, without raising or lowering his voice, or laying stress
on any particular portion. But why go on ? You are
right: the University seems to exist chiefly for the benefit
of a few grossly over-paid professors.
“ Fitzclarence ” on the Queensland political
situation :
Dear Bulletin, —The persistence with which the
Queensland "Government organ” and several pretty
well-informed hangers-on of the Plunder-party prophesy
the early withdrawal of Nelson from the leadership has a
good deal to justify it. It is almost a certainty that had
not the Queen-street drapery vote tumbled Tommy Byrnes,
the mantle of the chief would ere this have fallen on the
shoulders of his beloved lieutenant. The personal
acquaintances of the Lazy Knight can only account for his
continued vitality by the fact that he is not insensible to
the cajolery of public flattery, or that his desire for the
life of Boodlewraith makes him superior to the charms of
rest and rust. Byrnes, of course, won’t be taken at any
price. The party owes its solidarity to the presence of its
“ beloved chief.” Even with Nelson’s imprimatur it is
doubtful if the Attorney-General would get a majority of
the Govt, members, so that you will at once see through
the other man Tozer’s game. Even now Drake’s paper
is howling for Tozer —in preference to Byrnes. I doubt
if there is a more accomplished wire-puller in Australia
than H. Tozer. The results of, say, 12 months’ tinkering
with the Labor-party are: the partial conversion of
Glassey and Cross, and the “fixing” of little Frank
M’Donnell, of Fortitude Valley. And if the same subtle
serenading goes on much longer there is no saying how-
great a following would go with the “Minister for
Labor” in a new Tozer administration. On the other
hand, Byrnes has been proposed, and has been accepted
by Andy Dawson, the keen Charters Towers senior, who
will unquestionably influence a few of the “Twenty”
should the threatened disruption come. Maranoa King
(who was forced to declare his views on each plank of the
Labor platform seriatim on the eve of his election
because of his apparently weakening allegiance to the
reform policy) gave several votes dead against his party
last session, and he is therefore considered as a sure re-
cruit for one of the new factions. And then there is J.
Plumper Hoolan, whose latest moves are what, no fellow
can understand. He is in active warfare with the
Trades Hall people; is a doughty champion of the
Q.N. Bank, and has been knowm to bare his muscular
tongue in defence of the Ministry. The Boy from Burke
has given no whisper which could help one to discover
what new force is working with him, and, by general con-
sent, it is written down eccentricity. An all-wise Govt,
has sized the matter up cleverly enough, and John’s
eccentricity is to be given a chance to take definite shape
during a three months’ picnic on the mining commission.
Now, here are six cases in which “ the influence ” has pro-
duced outward and visible effect, and, apart from these
six, where will be found a man among the party with suf-
ficient nous to hold together the remaining forces ? The
game is being craftily played, and you may take it as a
prognostication that, ere many moons, Queensland Legis-
lature will consist of a Govt, and an Opposition, and that
the party of “ leaders,” sneeringly dubbed a “ faction ” by
Hugh Nelson, will have became the henchmen of the man
who has dropped his Liberal creed for one of mild Social-
ism, or the admirers of the adroit lawyer whom, in the
words of Dawson, “ You fellows don’t seem to under-
stand.”

The consensus of five Australian provinces in support

In placid little Aramac (Q.), the other day, 52
persons, each grabbing a roll of notes, fell over
each other in the narrow doorway of the local
land-office, to enter their application for one
grazing-farm. Their deposits totalled ,£6BOO,
or nearly £l3O a man. The hideous fun
is that the winner is decided by lottery. It is
a wild gamble from the start, in a land wherein
gambling is forbidden by law, and from enter-
ing which newspapers publishing gambling
advts. are barred. It is not always—or, rather,
it has not always been a clean and straight
gamble. Cases have been suspected, and some-
times actually known, where the lottery has
been faked, and the fakir has received a hand-
some present from the land-shark benefiting
by the manipulation of the machinery. The
alleged intention of the Act under which
these lots are drawn is that the poor
man and bond fide selector shall have as
good a chance as the rich man who
only wants to add to his acres, or as the specu-
lator who only desires to sell out at a premium
to the first eager bidder. The alleged intention
of the Act, therefore, is the same as the genuine
intention and actual results of “ Tattersall’s
Consultation,” banished from the St. Peters-
burg of Brisbane to the Siberia of Hobart.
In the Aramac case, there is very little
doubt that 46 out of the 52 applicants were not
bond-fide intending settlers, but merely gam-
blers on the chance of getting £5OO premium
on their luck. The Rockhampton (C.Q,.)
Record puts the proportion of these gamblers
at 80 per cent. ; but the writer adds 10 per
cent, for the Record’s obtrusive bias for
all things Centralboodlewraithian —including
alleged honesty.
“The Prince of Wales, referring to the intended cele-
bration in honor of the completion of the sixtieth year of
Her Majesty the Queen's reign, states that he feels assured
that amongst its features will be included some movement
in the direction of an effort to ameliorate the condition of
the poor throughout the Kingdom.”— Cable.
The good Judge went to London—
(The jolly black cap !)
The good Judge went to London—
(The Lord ha’ mercy !) ;
A bloody mist rose o’er us ;
Seven gibbets creaked in chorus ;
Lo ! Golgotha lay before us,
When the good Judge went to London.
The good Judge climbed the backstairs—
(ls the rope greased ?)
The good Judge climbed the backstairs—
(ls the prayer said ?)
A wind blew off Mount Rennie,
And the devil in his den, he
Swore and shivered more than any,
When the good Judge climbed the backstairs.
The good Judge knelt on the cold mat—
(How the bolt groaned !)
The good Judge knelt on the cold mat—
(How the blood leapt !)
The body for God’s acre
The wife begged ! . . . Sweet to take her
That image of his Maker,
When the good Judge knelt on the cold mat.
The good Judge fell with a dull thud—
(Cut down the carcase !)
The good Judge fell with a dull thud—
(Shovel the earth in !)
As that glad note resounded
Beneath the pavement mounded,
The merry black bones bounded
When the good Judge fell with a dull thud.
To a heartless greed for wealth the money-
making men are urged,
A greed that has no confines of satiety ;
They might show consideration for the groaning
tenth submerged
If pity paved the pathway to Society.
But gold alone will do it; they must feed a
dozen earls
And curry favors hollow and transparent,
When the object of the missis is to “ elevate ”
her girls
Through a visit from the royal Heir Apparent,
The world of rank, and fashion lias no room
within its ring
For cranks who would ameliorate conditions ;
Its parvenu inhabitants must grease the Coming
King
And uphold a money standard of “ positions. ”
From the Monarch in the centre, with nobilities
around,
The circles spread to plutocratic classes
Who are striving, ever striving, to get on to
holy ground
By the aid of profits sweated from the masses.
His Highness, Albert Edward, asks for efforts
to be made
In a modern socialistical direction :
He loves those folks who suffer “ in the interests
of trade ”
With an almost more than fatherly affection.
Does lie want to start a movement for the
benefit of man ?
Well, let him curse the Crown and Consti-
tution,
And bid the “ lower orders ” lend assistance to
his plan
In the only way they can—a Revolution.

At Cassilis station (Gulf country, Q.), a
poor devil who was bushed and dying of thirst,
“ went one better” than the S.A. overlander who
recently cut the telegraph-wire in order to summon
help. This one set a deserted bough-shed on fire;
but, when help was attracted to the place, he was
as dead as Ingersoll’s Satan.
