SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Lyceum “ True Irish Hearts.”
Royal “ Tommy Atkins.”
Hke Majesty’s “ Sinbad the Sailor.”
Criterion “A Woman’s Reason.”
Palace Goatcher’s Variety Co.
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “Matsa.”
Royal “ For England.”
Bijou “Adventures of Miss
Brown.”
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Alexandra “Not Guilty.”
The second production of the M‘Carthy
season, at Sydney Lyceum, “True Irish Hearts,”
does a hard and furious sprint for first place as
the dullest Irish drama going. The title fits
badly, to begin with ; the story is mostly Yankee,
and there is only one Irishman in it, that lone-
some broguey person being a flunkey in the em-
ploy of an American baronet, or at least an un-
accountable baronet in America. The play was
evidently built round this allegedly funny person ;
he comes in on his head amid a blizzard of broken
crockery, and thereafter he holds the centre of the
stage for long, weary periods while a servant-girl
asks him questions to give him an opportunity of
firing off the old Joe Millerism that is lying on
his chest. The questions are very much like those
that the middle man in the “ first part ” asks of
the burnt-cork nigger with the bones, and they fit
into the drama as neatly as if the prompter
brought in the book, and hunted up the place with
a damp forefinger and a wet thumb, and read off
the conundrum and then looked up the answer in
the index. The Irish-American “musical comedy
drama,” as it is nowadays, is an airy scrap of
fancy which frisks as lightly as the Hon. John
Lucas and a kedge anchor.
* * * *
Apart from the sere and yellow chestnut
of the Irish flunkey, there isn’t much drama.
Scot Inglis, who is a bad, unshaven character,
keeps turning up periodically to restore Miss
Harrie Ireland’s lost child in consideration of
much immediate bullion, but he generally restores
the wrong child. Miss Ireland lives in continuous
horror lest her baronial husband should get to
know of this child, though it was born in wedlock
and there is nothing wrong about it. An itinerant
German organ-grinder also arrives casually with
more lost child, and the baronet, who is a sort of
warm-hearted home for lost dogs, takes them all
in and adopts everybody. Scot Inglis mur-
ders the baronet rather publicly, without excit-
ing much attention. Finally, Inglis falls over
the wall of an Irish castle, and as he isn’t
quite dead the funny man is understood to
drop a boulder on him from a great height;
in the presence of several ladies and others, and
flatten him to death. Then Miss Ireland remarks
that the clouds have now passed away, or words
to that effect, and some one else states that sun-
shine is restored or thereabouts, and they all
agree that the funny man who has just dropped
the rock on Inglis is a noble, warm-hearted son of
Erin. Miss Ireland bestows her hand and her
immense wealth on the German organ-grinder,
who tries vainly to look like “ Hans the Boat-
man,” and the lost child is sorted out, and some
one rises up about this time to say that the gloom
having been thus lifted, he has invited the neigh-
boring peasantry to do a song and dance, where-
upon the weary jig eventuates as usual. The
generous Irish way in which the boulder is
dropped on Inglis, with the approval of the ladies
and everybody else, is the one really original
feature of the play. A number of people are
variously connected with this production, but The
Bulletin has no desire to make their misfortune
any more public* than is absolutely necessary.
** * *
The second edition of “ Sinbad ” was pat
op at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) last Saturday. It
contains a number of new songs, which are a little
brighter than the old lot, and a certain Miss Fin-
lay, who does Miss Bessie Clayton’s famous back-
kick as well as the great original contortionist—at
least the present writer sees very little difference.
Lucifer, the astonishing high-kickist, varies his
entertainment now by going into the dreary heavy-
weight business, and lifts with one hand a dumb-
bell weighing 2001 b. This is a sufficiently wonder-
ful performance, but for concentrated dreariness
the heavy-weight business has few equals, and
even if Lucifer lifted a dumb-bell weighing
2000 cwt. it would be difficult to view the circum-
stance with any great interest. Lucifer’s new
business isn’t all of the 2001 b. dumb-bell order,
however, and when he isn’t laboriously humping
large pieces of iron he is a highly interesting per-
former. To-morrow (Friday) a “grand athletic
night ” is announced in honor of the Lucifers,
when the people who furiously pursue the inflated
sphere with their boots, and the men who whack
the leather globe in the hope of knocking down
three pieces of wood, and the men who get on to a
flat piece of ground and run desperately after
nothing with most of their clothes off, and many
other people, will be present.
** * *
The present programme is doing its last
nights at Sydney Royal, though “Tommy Atkins”
in his fourth week goes with the same vigor as of
yore. Vigor and go are the most conspicuous points
about “ Tommy Atkins.” It is full of large,
robust men who seem to grasp the situation with
both hands, and the spectacle of the Rev. Private
Plimmer rushing to battle is in itself good value
for 4s. Plimmer rushes to battle in a way that
calls for high praise ; and taking people and things
all round, the advt. is less of an Ananias or a
bank auditor—which is practically the same thing
—than usual when it calls the Royal crowd “ the
best dramatic co. in Australia.”
** * *
“A Woman of No Importance ” at Sydney
Criterion ends her rather unimportant career on
Friday. “ A Woman’s Reason ” follows on
Saturday.
A faint suspicion that Knowles has used
up his best jokes and is now getting through his
second-best lot pervades Sydney Palace. Quite a
lot of his latest humors are obtrusi vely venerable,
and one awakes recognition as a Bulletin pro-
duction which this paper gave way to in a weak
moment about ten years ago. Little Miss
Antonio, the three Delevines, and those two
graceful athletes, Wilton and Stack, however,
keep up their reputation for freshness and
originality, and cover a multitude of things, even
including the two people who meet accidentally in
a lunatic asylum and sing to each other, the lady
singist having bright green stockings on. And as
Henry Lee’s realistic representation of Parkes
may be fairly set against the commonplace of two
very ordinary song-and-dance men things are pro-
bably about even.
* * * *
Chirgwin and Charles Godfrey continue to
be the mainstays of Sydney Tivoli, which, so far,
doesn’t appear to be blighted worth a cent by the
opposition show at the Palace. The blight is
pretty well distributed among the big theatres
instead, with rather more of it at the Lyceum
than elsewhere. All the three cinematographes
seem to be doing well enough, and James
M'Mahon’s “ein.”in Pitt-street makes an extra
effort this week with a gorgeous colored view
of the great fountains at Versailles, which piece
of pictorial glitter is about the best thing up to
date.
* * * *
The Edison Vitascope, at 162 Pitt-street,
Sydney, is the Cinematographe Edison-wise. It
has such an attractive programme, with music and
color effects thrown in, that it fetched the clergy,
including two bishops, along the other afternoon
for a special half-hour, and in that thirty minutes
the church soaked up more graceful Annabelle,
more boxer Corbett, more May Irwin’s kissing,
more Amy Muller’s toe-dance, more dog-fight,
more knife-duel, more buck-jump riding, more
bar-room scene, more dance with skirt, and more
dance without skirt than it ever got in at any
previous day and night of its existence. The
Vitascope, therefore, supplies a long-felt want to
people of the highest motives, and the shapeliness
of the many dancers’ legs is a moving lesson in
the triumph of the mind over the body.
* * -* *
Miss Beatrice Griffiths’ farewell-concert
takes place at Sydney Town Hall on the 26th
Governor Hampden and other dignitaries of
great social tonnage propose being present to assist
the songstress on her way, and many good names
are on the programme.
* * * *
Melbourne shows are much the same as
last week, with the exception of a new horror at
the Alexandra, where the Idiot who flapped his
fins, and said he knew what was going on, has
been superseded by “Not Guilty.” In this case,
the dramatic unities suffered last Saturday from a
general neglect of the company to rush forward at
the right moment. The stage was sometimes
deserted just when the plot ought to have been
thickening, and the curtain seemed on one occa-
sion to descend in a drunk and incapable
manner without waiting for instructions; but,
take it altogether, “Not Guilty” was prefer-
able to the Idiot aforesaid. In addition to about
a dozen principals who appeared to make up their
parts as they went along, it was supported by
three supernumerary convicts at Dartmoor. These
turned up again later as a British army in India.
Also, there was an inexplicable widow. She died
of starvation previous to revelling in enormous
wealth, whereas Augustus Glover, as the villain,
fell a victim to drink ere he took to burglary and
became chief warder at Dartmoor. Glover’s
great aim was to ruin Wilfrid Shine for the
mere sake of witnessing that harmless hero’s
grief, and simply because Shine had given
him a shilling to slake his thirst with, con-
sequently “Not Guilty” might pass as a ser-
mon against promiscuous charity if it were a
more reliable entertainment. But since Saturday,
the company have probably found a few missing
motives, and more likely than not Shine is now a
villain in pursuit of innocent Glover on a charge
of having stolen the widow’s dying child. The
Alexandra struggles include several intelligent,
experienced people who could do very well at im-
provising dialogue around one strong, central idea.
A gruesome, up-to-date idea is all they require.
The Bulletin would suggest an incoherent
mining drama, with the Ballarat leper for princi-
pal chai'acter.
* * * *
“ Matsa,” at the Princess’s, retains all its
early magnificence, and improves steadily in
other respects. The discovery of a pigmy race
in Africa has already given a strange air of
truth to Little Gulliver and his tribe. A cable-
gram announcing that the Nile is on the rise may
be expected to arrive shortly. Anyway, the Yarra
has risen a good deal during the last fortnight,
therefore the inhabitants of various suburbs where
the kitchen fire gets drowned these damp times
are much concerned about the fate of Matsa’s
naming ballet when the flowing tide comes in.
* * * *
“ Miss Brown” fills the Bijou comfortably,
as usual. Frank Thornton, spinster, will shake
his gory locks at the boarding-school, and fight
the unconvincing detective to a finish in the cow-
shed for another week or two, after which he
leaves for Sydney. The Bijou, by the way, owes
a debt of gratitude to Thornton for having restored
its long-lost popularity by means of a comedy that
makes women laugh without bringing a blush to
the comparatively modest cheeks of their male-
kind. Some of the secret of “Miss Browns”
success must lie in its stern determination not to
be shocking.
* * * *
At Melbourne Royal the remarkable fox-
hunt, likewise the alleged defeat of the wild Boer
are proceeding until further notice. People have
become inured to Walter Baker’s frequent out-
bursts concerning Hingland, and every time the
Glorious Flag makes itself a nuisance they regard
that awful bit of bunting with toleration for the
sake of Bland Holt’s scenery and effects. Miss
Watson being away on sick leave for a few
days, Miss Vivienne has taken her place
in Baker’s stage affections, whilst Miss Grahame
wears the drab, despondent garments of the
girl who went astray. This seems a suitable
time for requesting the management to explain
why its gone-wrong damsels invariably carry the
same brand of shame on their limp walking cos-
tumes—but no matter. Miss Grahame expresses
sorrow for her simple little error in a natural sort
of way, and the fact that she is a slim little lady
with a frail voice amounts to a plea for public
sympathy. This is a cruel world for any poor
young thing who can’t make a formidable show
against the vile majah when he brings his glitter-
ing eye into action.
* * # *
The Allisons joined Rickards’ force at the
Opera House on Saturday last, and danced them-
selves into considerable favor. Carl Hertz was to
re-appear on Tuesday, for a short season, and the
only Coleman hasn’t gone away. Also, Alice
Leamar’s festive appearance as a red-cross nurse
continues to rob warfare of its terrors for anybody
who would reckon a mortal wound cheap at the
price, if Alice were at hand to take him into her
little tent and kiss the place to make it well.
At Poverty Point.
Dear Bulletin, — Of the many wild and incomprehen-
sible things done in melodrama, the wildest and incom-
prehensiblest is the way the English language is spoken.
There appears to be a special brand of English turned on
there with the red-and-pale-white limelight. I have just
been to see a melodrama, and find it no exception to
the rule. The heroine continually refers to “may hort,”
which means “my heart”; while she rolls her “ fayne
aze,” which is melodramese for “fine eyes.” She says
“may chayld” when she refers to her unfortunate off-
spring. When the hero or the villain says “ Yah 1 ” he is
not derisive : he only means “ hear” in English. There is
an impression in “may maynd” (which, of course, is
my mind) that this jargon is supposed to be associated
with the aecents of the British aristocracy, and is very
“ refayned ” English. I can only say that I prefer the
broad Yorkshire-Scotch mixture of Henry Irving, or the
easy, fluent Yankee of Nat. Goodwin, to that sickening
and impossible speech of the melodramatic stage. And
what the devil do those two clever young male Australians
want “ yahing ” and “ thahtng ” about the stage ? “ May
Gahd, sar, aye lev har dearah than may layfe,” is enough
to give an Australian with a musical ear what one of those
actahs himself would probably call the “graypes”!—
Diogenes.
Actor Bandmann visited Australia and then wrote the
inevitable book. What struck him about Brisbane was
the prevalence of thunderstorms. He says : “Itis no
uncommon thing to see a cab-horse bolting down the
street, the driver having been struck by lightning.”
Melb.: “Delightful old Spiritualist Peebles is back,
the Spectre Bridegroom of the craft, in rusty black, a
‘ Matsa ’ mummy, exhaling the sweet odor of a sandal-
wood coffin. On his last visit, I reported him one Sunday
night, at Melb. Opera House, Chas. Bright chairman.
Bright said, ‘We will now sing a hymn, by Pope.’ An
Irish policeman, on duty with another, near me, turned
to his mate and asked, ‘ Hwhat Poap’s that ? Not Poap
Leo, Oi’ll be bound.’ ”
Melb.: “I don’t know how it is, but they can’t make
‘ Matsa ’ a Johnny piece. Its salacity is most appreciated
by mothers, being ample Gampy-Saxon, not flippety
Gallic enough for Johnny. He just wants to stroke his
walking-cane gently against a brand-new leg.”
Mofussil : “ Gabriel, the freak, the undoubted black
man turned white, exhibiting in Melb., reminds me of
Anglo-Indians, who have turned almost, or quite, the hue
of Hindoos. This is attributable to the action of heat on

Nat Goodwin, as was expected, found things rather
warm for him on his return to America. Miss Walsh and
the volatile Dorothy Usner, who dropped out of his Aus-
tralian company when the sisters Elliott came along, re-
turned home with unfavorable reports of Nathaniel’s
doings in the Southern Hemisphere, and now Dorothy is
suing her late manager for libel in that he did explain
her discharge from the co. as being due to incompetence
and indecent conduct. As regards Miss Blanche Walsh,
The Bulletin predicted that she would give Goodwin a
nasty jar. She was the toney lady of the crowd, engaged
to play lead, and at the last moment before leaving
America, her mother suggested to Goodwin that the con-
tract had better be cancelled, seeing that he had then
made arrangements for Maxine Elliott to follow the ex-
pedition. Goodwin, however, anxious not to quarrel
with the Walshes, assured them that Maxine’s advent
would not affect the position of the original leading lad.v.
But it did, of course. As soon as the lovely one came
upon the scene she objected to Miss Walsh’s name “ star-
ring” in the advts. of “ Romeo’s First Love,” and a week
later the young lady sailed back to the Land of the Free
with her ma.
The several versions of the successful Fax and the un-

“ What always strikes me is the pathos of the pit,” says
a Hinglish cynic. “That people should patiently wait
outside for an hour or two in the cold to see some of the
shows now being played in London, brings tears to my
sympathetic eyes.” Tiik Bulletin used to think that the
early door for “ A Trip to Chinatown ” was a most affect-
ing spectacle.
The old familiar voice of Newton Griffiths, as an Egyp-
tian person in “ Matsa,” awakens memories of the time
when he used to relate real and imaginary Deeming
atrocities to gaping crowds at Melbourne Waxworks.
Then was Griffiths in his true element. Every week he
would add a new chapter to the awful story, until at last
he stood knee-deep, as it were, in the blood of victims
whom Deeming had never heard of.
May Pollard’s glittering stage jewels are a feature of
“Matsa.” These are her own personal props, a present
from her brother in India, and Miss May feels worth at
least a million sterling when she appears with ’em all on.
It was “ Visor,” of London Weekly Dispatch, who
described Johnel Toole’s Antarctic expedition to Aus-
tralia as a “ glittering triumph.” Now “ Visor ” smooths
the way for Chas. Godfrey’s return, thus : “ I hear that,
notwithstanding his great success at the Antipodes, Mr.
Charles Godfrey is getting a little homesick, and may,
therefore, be expected to be back in London at Easter.”
“ A little homesick ”is distinctly good. But what a com-
fort it sometimes is for the cawlinies that some of the
imported articles have a home to get sick about.

Haresfoot: “Successful dramas written and produced
in Australia : Cooper’s ‘ Sunlight and Shadow’ —but that
is not exactly the title ; Garnet Walch’s ‘ Robbery Under
Arms,’ about three of Darrell’s, two out of four or five
adaptations of ‘ His Natural Life.’ I know not if there
be any other that achieved more than a success of esteem,
such as the Vagabond’s ‘No Mercy,’ Bunster’s ‘ Class,’
Marcus Clarke’s ‘ Plot,’ and so forth. Hardly anyone is
aware that an Age journalist, Jaffray, had an original
blank verse tragedy, ‘ The Gladiator,’ produced at Melb.
Royal over 30 years ago.”
H. : “A survey of the all-but-unbroken roll of Christ-
mas pantomimes staged at the Melb. Royal for 41 years—
the only gap was Lyster’s Grand Opera, with Weber’s
Oberon) in 1865—reveals Akhurst, Marcus Clarke, Garnet
Walch, Finn, and other authors, who had the opportunity
of putting in more body than nowadays, since none had
so much expensive paraphernalia crammed in as ‘ Matsa.’
The ear was tickled a bit, while now the eye is made
drunk. Akhurst, who was Herald sub-editor, had a
public sensitive to local history, wherefore his ‘ House
That Jack Built,’ 1869, bore the second title of ‘The Loves
of Jack Melbourne ’ and ‘ Little Victoria,’ while Hennings’
inevitable panorama entranced the audience, with Fawk-
ner’s schooner up the Yarra, on the forest site of the
city.”
Actor Nesbitt’s grave, tombstoned by G. V. Brooke, in
Geelong cemetery, has been beautifully restored, by sub-
scription. They never met, but were much alike.
In the hunting-scene at Melb. Royal the riders make
their first run from Little Bourke-st. After dropping
down over the miniature waterfall and jumping the
hurdle at the “ wing,” they emerge into the lane that runs
by the side of the theatre, and so back to Lilly Bourke-
st., whence they make a second journey through the
stage-door, and do it all over again. Probably there is no
other theatre in the world where an equestrian troupe
could go hunting with the same effect.

The Cloneurry country (N.Q.) yields the
latest hideous yarn of death from thirst in the
bush. Sheep-drover John Forbes got stuck up for
water. He sent his man, Thomas Martin, to
search for it. Martin found it, but allegedly
failed to return to Forbes. (The unforgivable
offence m “outside” country.) Forbes’s body
was found dead, with the clothing torn off. Why
men dying of thirst often strip off their clothes
has never been scientifically theorised out. The
bush-traces eloquently told that Forbes had killed
a sheep and drunk its blood. His horse was found
dead four miles from his corpse, and 24,000 sheep
perished ! Another poor devil was found dead in
a dry creek-bed, within half-a-mile of water, only
a few miles from Hughenden.

The Wild-Cat Column.
Treasurer Fysh of Tasmania, deserves a high
place among those whose assurance passes all understand-
ing. Recently The Bulletin drew attention to the fact
that in one year (1895) Tasmania spent *113,000 of bor-
rowed money, and nearly £lll,OOO of this was for works
3' l elded n .° direct return, and which, therefore
should be paid for out of revenue instead of out of
loans From these figures (v h are to be found in
Coghlan s statistics) this papm u-ew the inference that
nance was le us ual kind of swindle, and
that the Tasmanian surplus was the usual kind of bogus
surplus, and that Fysh was the usual kind of Treasurer
that these provinces know so well-only a little more
usual than the average. Treasurer Fysh, at a recent
election meeting:, rose to deny the above fio- ure q tn
say that not £lll,OOO, but less than £3OOO of loan moneys
had gone in non-revenue-producing works in the year
alluded to. Then he produced a list in support
°L S °u le , case - The loan expenditure, as
shown by Fysh, included £54,500 for roads, bridges
dredging- works, &c. ; £3700 for schools ; £4BOO
for miscellaneous public buildings; £1386 for mis-
cellaneous public works including such things
as a quarantine-ground and an embankment beside
a swamp ; £37,300 for loans to local bodies ; and £9lOO
borrowed to cover the expenses of borrowing: a previous
£750,000. These are Fysh’s own figures, and yet Fysh
alleges that only £3OOO of the expenditure was non-
revenue-producing.
Fysh distinctly hangs himself with his own
figures. The road-money expenditure doesn’t produce
any direct revenue, and there is no certain evidence that
it produced any revenue indirectly. Roads are necessary,
like police and soldiers and gaols and lunatic asylums and
many other things, but the fact that they are'necessary
doesn t show that they return even one shilling per cent,
on their cost to the public Treasury. The schools, public
buildings, and miscellaneous works don’t pay the Trea-
sury either. There is no earthly hope of the loan floated
to pay the expenses of floating a previous loan ever
returning any dividends. The only item in Fysh’s con-
tradiction which doesn’t prove the absolute truth of Tub
Bulletin’s statement is the £37,300 for loans to local
bodies, and this is really almost as shady an item as the
rest. The local bodies borrow largely for unproductive
works which should be paid for out of revenue. They
raise the interest by taxation and pay it to the Treasury
—when they do pay it—and the Treasury pays it to the
original lender. The result to the community at large is
just the same in the end as if the Government built the
unproductive works itself and paid the interest out of
taxation. But, leaving this item out, Treasurer Fysh
clearly shows that his Government “blewed” £73,000 in
one year on non-productive works, and after showing
that he did this he denies that he did it.
In this particular direction Tasmania has the
Worst record in all Australia. Victoria spent on roads,
bridges, dredging, fortifications, and assisted immigration
less than one-fiftieth of its total borrowed money ; N.S.W.
spent one-ninth ; Queensland fully one-fifth ; S.A. one-
sixth ; Westralia one-fifth ; Tasmania no less than one-
third. This is saying nothing of a mysterious outlay
classed as “ miscellaneous,” and Tasmania has spent
a larger proportion of its borrowed money on “mis-
cellaneous” than any other Australian province. Only
half of Tasmania’s borrowings has gone in reproductive
works ; the rest has been spent to save the magnates of
the land of Lots of Sleep from being taxed sufficiently to
make the country pay its way. The result is that the
interest on the public debt in Tas. swallows up a far larger
proportion of the total revenue than in any other province;
that more of the interest has to be made up out of taxa-
tion than in any other province, because less of it is
revenue-producing ; and that the province has to fake its
accounts even worse than the others by spending a larger
proportion of its new borrowings on works which should
be paid for out of revenue, in order to disguise the fact
that it is even more insolvent than its neighbors. And
no amount of Fysh can get away from these facts.
* * * *
Sydney municipality has just floated a per
cent, loan of £150,000 locally at 99. The best price at
which the Sydney Council has ever borrowed in London
was 4 per cent., and a considerable amount of its British
indebtedness carries 5 and 5J per cent, interest. Conse-
quently the local market pans out remarkably well by
comparison. Mayo? Ives reckons that even if London
would have taken a per cent, loan at par (1 per cent,
above the local price), when flotation expenses and the
cost of remitting interest are considered the results would
show a balance of £5693 in favor of the local market —
which means 3s. per cent, per annum saved by the present
method.
* * * *
A brief telegram from Brisbane says that the
Queensland National Bank has postponed its half-yearly
meeting until after the reconstruction proposals are laid
before the shareholders—probably some time in February.
It is vaguely reported that the arrangement is getting on
all right; and, meanwhile, it appears that the half-year
has ended with a loss of £1607. As every other half-year
since the reconstruction ended with an alleged profit, this
is presumably the result of the recent investigation.
When the overhaul took place it was reported that a con-
siderable amount of the profits were bogus—arrived at by
charging in the books interest which couldn’t be recovered
on advances which were either lost or partly lost, and
reckoning the same as if actually received. If the present
telegraphed results are any criterion, it would seem that
the net profits were wholly bogus ; at all events, the last
balance-sheet before that investigation showed an alleged
net profit of over £lO,OOO, which was not much below the
average, and the first one after it shows a sudden drop of
£11,600 and a dead loss. Whether the £1607 is the whole
loss, or whether the board is still taking a sanguine view
of things and there are more revelations to follow, is
another question. The board talks of submitting the re-
construction scheme to the shareholders and getting their
approval, but very little is said about how the approval of
the depositors—who are the really interested parties—is
panning out. If they, and especially the British section
of them, don’t approve, the shareholders amount to
nothing.

ment securities and Treasury notes show £275,000 of cash
°. r i 1? equivalent in hand —equal to 33 per cent, of all

another instalment of its reconstructed deposits, making
in all 50 per cent, released, and the money has been re-
deposited so freely at a lower rate of interest that the

while the liquid assets have also increased very largely.
Considering its remarkably strong position it seen s cer-
tain the Royal could set free the other half of its recon-
structed money in one act without the slightest risk, and
thus gain considerably in prestige while making a solid
saving in interest.

The new Victorian Companies Act, despite all
the emasculation effected by the Leg. Council, is a
measure of some value, and the action of the Turner
Government m accepting what there was of it in the hope
getting more later on, instead of standing on its
ignity and wrecking the whole measure, seems reason-
abie enough. Under the new order of things, auditors
must be licensed as an evidence of competence ; no person
can act as auditor if he owes money to the institution
audited ; no director or other employs can be an auditor
while in the co. s employ or for 12 months after leaving
it, and auditors are made responsible for the accuracy of
the books, whereas heretofore they have only been called
upon to state that the balance-sheet agrees with the books
without saying whether the books agree with facts or not.
the balance-sheet must show the amount of paid-up capi-
tal,, and how much is paid up respectively in cash or
p/n s or other alleged securities; amounts due to the
co., after allowance is made for bad and doubtful debts ;
whether assets are taken at cost price or at valuation ;
the amount written off for depreciation ; the amount, if
an -V, written-up on the assets; how the reserve-fund is
invested, and sundry other things. Any objection made
by the auditors is to be reported to the shareholders, in-
stead of privately to the directors, as has generally been
done heretofore. No company is allowed to have any lien
on its own shares, except for calls, which breaks up the
practice of companies lending on their own shares.
Loans to managers, auditors, or employes, or firms
in which they have an interest, are prohibited, like-
Wls ® ttie payment of dividends except out of
profits. The amount of any mortgage upon the
uncalled capital must be published, and the assets or the
uncalled capital cannot be mortgaged except by resolu-
tion of the shareholders. Directors are prohibited from
selling their own properties to their own companies—a
regulation which, if it had existed 10 or 12 years ago,
would have knocked the bottom out of nearly every boom
land-bank in Melbourne. No institution can, in future,
call itself a bank or savings bank unless it has at least
£200,000 subscribed and £75,000 paid-up capital, and is
also specially authorised by Parliament to use the title.
Also, no company can commence business or borrow
money until at least one-twelfth of its capital is paid up.
There are a number of other provisions, but these are
the ones which are most calculated to make the company-
boomer sit up suddenly.
* * * .
The City Bank of Sydney reports that its net
profits for the half-year ended 31st December are £BO7l
against £BlOB, £10,019, £10,030, and £10,004 for four pre-
vious six-monthly periods. A 4 per cent, dividend
absorbs £BOOO, and the remaining £7l, added to £BlBB
previously on hand, leaves £8259 to carry forward. The
quaint feature about the City Bank’s profit balance is that
it is always just enough to pay an even dividend and leave
a very few pounds over—so very few that the coincidence
is altogether remarkable. In the days when a 5 per
cent, dividend absorbed £lO,OOO, the earnings were so
exactly £lO,OOO that the total surplus for three half-
years onlj r amounted to £54. When the dividend went
down to 4 per cent. (£8000), the profits also dropped so
accurately that the surplus in two half-years is only
£179. Barring the fact that 4 per cent, is a ver3 r small
dividend, and that if the steady shrinkage continues
even it will soon be a matter of difficulty, and that the
balance-sheet gives the smallest amount of information
on record, there is nothing specially bad about the
present report. Deposits and note circulation
have increased slightly. Cash on hand has in-
creased by no less than £71,000, and now amounts
to the large sum of £334,ooo—equal to over 30
per cent, of the liabilities. That vasty item of
obscurity, “ Bills and all other Debts due to the
Bank,” which accounts for everything that isn’t cash or
office premises, remains about the same. The City Bank
is a small concern which never seems to get any larger or
any more vigorous, and as it has only got £406 ahead in
3J4 years, its career seems pretty uneventful. During
the same period the reserve has gone down from £219,000
to £lOO,OOO, and the prospect of ever getting it up again
at the present rate of progress isn’t worth speaking about.

The alleged advantages of private enterprise in
the matter of railway-building are again illustrated in
Westralia, where the Government has bought the Great
Southern line from its embarrassed owners for £1,100,000.
The line thus costs the State £4525 per mile in cash.
The co. also received nearly 3,000,000 acres of land, and
if this was worth even 7s. an acre the whole cost to the
State is £9050 a mile. The lines built by the State cost
on an average £3804 per mile. Therefore, the advantages
of leaving the work to the much-lauded private enterprise
are that it cost about £1,200,000 extra; that it was a
ceaseless worry, and a subject of political intrigue ; that
the private co. went broke, whereas the Govt, railway
S3'stem in W.A. not only pays working expenses and in-
terest, but 3 T ields an actual profit; and that the fares on
the private line while it was in the company's hands were
higher than the rates charged by the State. (As examples:
Government lines—Perth to Bunbury, 115 miles, second-
class fare, 125.; private lines —Midland Junction to Coom-
berdale, 110 miles, second-class fare, 13s. 9d.; Beverley to
Yaraben, 113J4 miles, second-class fare, 14s. 3d.) There
doesn’t seem to be anything great in all this for private
enterprise to brag about. A private railway under purely
local management and run with local capital might
pay. The private railway which is bossed in London
never does, for directors, managers, experts, boodlers,
first-robbers, office expenses, commissions, brokerages,
and all manner of other swindles in London cost more
than the railway itself.

The former partner in a very big Australian
wine and spirit firm has just “gone ’’ in London for £9OOO
odd. Assets, £36. Since 'B4 the gentleman is stated to
have been drawing companies'directors fees at the rate of
£6OO a year. Directors must be getting scarce in London
lately. The dukes, generals, and admirals who used to
live on the guinea-pig industry must have all made their
piles on Westralian mine-flotations and retired from the
profession.
* * * *
The change that has taken place in the charac-
ter of ore available and in the methods of treatment
adopted at Broken Hill is shown by comparing the
exports for the last three years. Thus
. , ' 1894. 1895. 1896.
Ore and concentrates £644,896 £587,255 £953,861
Bulll °n £1,543,038 £959,562 £788,530
Totals ... ... £2,187,934 £1,546,817 £1,742,391
The totals do not show quite the full value of the mineral
exports, because a quantity of copper matte, and in some
years a small quantity of silver ingots, have been sent
away. But the figures do show the rapidity with which
the smelting ores have declined in value, partly owing to
the fall in the price of silver and partly owing to the
reduced metallic value of the ore ; at the same time,
though, they show the rapid increase during 1896 in the
export of ore and concentrates—mostly now, of course,
sulphides. During 1897 the value of the bullion is not
likely to fall below £600,000 (though 1898 may see a much
bigger fall), while the value of ore and concentrates is
likely to go to close upon £1,400,000. The Central alone,
on present appearances, will add a-quarter of a million to
the total.
* * * *
Silverton Tram Co. has entered into an agree-
ment for a period with the Barrier companies, and has
followed the S.A. railways in substantial reductions for
the carriage of carbonate ore and coal (from 4s. Bd. to
3s. 3d), sulphide ore and concentrates, firewood, salt,
flux, lead, and matte (4s. to 3s. 3d.); bullion (7s. 9d. to
65.). Freights of coke and mine-timber remain the same,
ss. 2d. per ton.

A new Pinnacles Group Co. is floated to make
still another attempt on a mine whose big bodies of rich
ore have hitherto been worse than valueless on account
of the presence of pyrrhotine in large quantities.

* * * *
Much —a little too much —is being printed about
the wonderful prospects of the Mt. David mine, formerly
known as the Mt. Maude, near Rockley, N.S.W. Total
stone crushed to date : 105 tons for 2700 z. Average,
2oz. lldwt. per ton. Good enough if it can be maintained,
and it will probably want it all to pay on a capital of
£120,000. The fact that Messrs. Knox and Schlapp had a
working bond on this property for several months and yet
did not buy it, is not considered worth mentioning in the
glowing descriptions the public will soon tire of reading.
Let the battery do the talking.
Ructions are promised at the next B.H. South
meeting. The Adelaide push want the management—
undercurrent. Block 14 is starving for the South ore,
and cannot work its game there till the cute Melb. crowd
is stifled. Block 14 has no more British ore to make
divs. from, and the South, properly engineered in the
skypilot city, would make another sweet lamb to skin.
* » * *
Junction North’s new plant, capable of weekly
jigging 1300 to 1400 tons as against the ruling. 600 ton out-
put, is billed for an early March move. With bigger
yields the shilling div. booms. The J.N. feels very well
with all of £23,000 at call. Shares held : Melbourne,
81,000 ; Adelaide, 31,175 ; London, 10,750 ; Sydney, 7025
—always at the tail-end of the good things.
* * * *
Perhaps the most promising N.S.W. gold-mine,
says a local correspondent, is the Royal Standard, at
Stewart’s Brook, one of the tributaries of the Upper
Hunter. It has broken two companies, but the present
seems to be destined to make up for past losses. The
reef has been worked for 500 ft. from the surface down-
wards, at which level it was met by an adit, driven from
the foot of the hill, and gave from loz. to 7oz. per ton.
In the upper levels, the reef was small and irregular, in
hard diorite formation, whioh, near the adit, turns into
kindly black slate, carrying an 18in. reef. A winze is now
being sunk from the adit, exposing a rich body of stone.
Last month’s crushing of 110 tons yielded over soz. per
ton, but, as the stone contains large quantities of arseni-
cal pyrites, and is treated at an ordinary battery, without
concentrating or grinding appliances, the concentrates
may be reckoned to be worth at least another soz. per ton.
* * * *
The official Vic. gold-return for 1896 is 805,0870 z.
—65,0010 z. above the yield for 1895, and the largest
since 1882; it exceeds by 228,6880 z. the 1891 return.
In other words, gold to the value of £900,000
more was obtained during the last twelve months
than for the corresponding period five years ago, and the
yield for 1897 is expected to total more than that of any
one of the previous 20 years, and to approach, if not
surpass, that of 1876, when the quantity won was over
900,0000 z. The quantity won during the last quarter
alone in Victoria was only between 10,000 oz. and 15,0000 z
less than the total estimated yield in Westralia for the
whole of 1896. The total Vic. gold-yield since 1851 is esti-
mated at 60,991,4080 z., equal in value to £243,865,632.
* * * *
The 180 is once more readying up to fill Bendigo
reefer Lansell’s profit eye. Payable milling from the
extension of the Vic. quartz reef at 2500 ft., while the
3200 ft. crosscut has discovered a Oft. reef and a 25ft.
thick spurry formation that promises well. Odds are that
Lansell will pile up a second £lOO,OOO from the 180.
Deserves it all. Since the last reef petered, deep sinking
and exploring has mopped up close on £70,000. Where is
there another man in Australia who would placidly sink
that sum in prospecting a region as hot as sheol?
* * * *
Johnson’s Reef (Bendigo) sees a more hopeful
future. Foot reef on the Nelson shaft should mill well
this term, and thus ease stockers of the heavy expense
in sinking the No. 1 shaft the 500 ft. to go to permit
intersecting the Windmill Hill reef. The adjacent
Princess Dagmar also has a decent prospect this half. A
crosscut at 2235 ft. expects to pick up the reef, and if
that is so, a lower one at 2335 ft. will prove its quality.
Better indications, too, at the 780 ft. level.
* * * *
Hustler’s shows (Bendigo) are in trouble. Bar-
ring Lansell’s Comet, the only div. mine of the group is
the United Hustlers, and that doesn’t seem to have a
much longer profit life in tow. Hustler’s No. 1, about
which the Sabbath tipster used to whoop, has had a dull
time, and the co. is just getting into line to develope the
Comet run of dirt. The old Hustler’s Reef out of luck.

* « * *
Prospectus of Melbourne United Gold Mining
Co. appears in this issue for “information only.” The
property, consisting of 18 acres, adjoining the White
Horse and Queen Margaret (W.A.), was floated privately
by Randolph Bedford, and, judging from the haste with
which certain wide-awake people chipped into the new
spec, as soon as it had “ gone off,” the M.U.G. M. Co. has
a future of some sort. A market future, or a divi-
dend ditto, or both. The show starts with favorable
reports from the managers of the Margaret and the Ivan-
hoe, and £BOOO working capital. An early crushing (by
arrangement with the Bulong Co.) of some 1500 tons
already available, is, it is asserted, more or less likely to
go 3oz. or better ; and if it does, shareholders may count
upon a long immunity from calls.
Melbourne United directorate has three names
that spell luck and attract punters—H. H. Drysdale and
Alec Campbell (of the Ivanhoe), and Agar Wynne, M.L.0.,
who is taking up a lot of shares. Randolph Bedford,
being the happy father of 32,000 shares, joins the board
on allotment, and, if the property comes up to expecta-
tions, that many-sided young human whirlwind will
probably issue the first half-yearly report in verse. Two
distinct persons—the mine-promoter and the Australian
bard—are ever fighting for supremacy beneath Randolph
B.’s shirt front, so it will all depend upon which of them
may be uppermost at the critical moment.
After the aforesaid float comes Melbourne
United North, which is about to be launched in Sydney
on Federation-market principles, The Bulletin supposes.
This sort of thing tends to create a community of interest
between the two provinces, although it may not always
produce much real interest for the communities.

* * * *
According to Q. Government Geologist Jack,
“ the mountains of purely specular and magnetio iron
ore” that he noticed in the Cloncurry district (Gulf
country) would yield “ colossal fortunes " to their owners
if the said mines were in England. When Cloncurry is
liberated from its present isolation by railway communi-
cation with either Cairns, Townsville, or Normanton, its
day of prosperity will come. It has long been known as
the richest metalliferous district in all Queensland. But
it has been nearly all collared in advance, although the
“Gridiron Land-grant Map” is not yet an accomplished
fact.

The social column of a recent Melb. Herald
begins:—
By the sad accident at Dunkeld, which resulted in the
death of Mrs. Silas Harding, Melbourne society loses one
of its wealthiest members. The deceased lady was ex-
pected to entertain very largely during the coming
season.
That gratitude which is a lively sense of favors to
come obviously prompted the kindly notice of the
obituarist, who yet hopes to be invited to a garden-
party in the Isles of the Blest, under the patronage
of the Archangels and their aides-de-camp. The
moral is that, if you are rich and are prompted to
entertain the Nicest people, don’t. Instead, give
blankets to the blacks and distribute tobacco at
the Benevolent Asylum.
THAT TERRIBLE INFANT.
DORIS (aged 4 ): “The dressmaker can’t get my new dress to fit me. What do you do, Uncle
Charlie, when she can’t get your trousers to jit properly?”—(Unde Charlie asks
hurriedly for the “ Mustard , please!”)
Geelong, where old Silas Harding built up
his colossal reputation for stinginess, seems to
have reaped considerable benefit from his estate,
after all. The local hospital committee has
decided to erect a tablet commemorating the late
(and second) Mrs. Harding’s charitable acts to the
institution. The first Mrs. Harding—poor crea-
ture ! —is not commemorated anywhere, except in
ancient anecdotes of the bad time she enjoyed
with Silas, when he was a comparatively young
and vigorous skinflint. One story relates that her
husband, forced to bring the doctor to her because
she couldn’t continue her daily round of domestic
slavery, was advised to supply her with more food.
Whereupon Silas went forth scowling to buy a
pound of steak and other necessaries for the
patient partner of his fortunes. No matter, the
second wife’s lavish expenditure must have made
his soul wriggle a bit.

* • * «
The irony of it: “A number of ladies
visited the Benevolent Asylum and Home
of Hope, and an enjoyable time was spent by
the inmates.” Most of the long-nosed ladies
who visit ’omes and worry the unfortunates
for full particulars and detailed accounts of
their “ fall ” are calculated to give an enjoyable
time to anybody. But then the miserable inmate
can’t rise and explain frankly how much she
wishes that all the crowd was in Gehenna. The
inspected one has to look joyful under all manner
of circumstances.

* * * *
A sidelight was lately cast on the ways
and means of the Australian metropolitan “mis-
sions ” during the hearing of a vagrancy charge at
Melb. City Court. A respectable woman, who
had vainly endeavored to get work, was found
lying on the steps of a church, and on her being
ushered into court, one of the sisters of the Wes-
leyan mission came forward and offered to take
the poor woman into their home. The woman
concurred, and the bench was about to order a
discharge when the “ nun ” remarked that the rule
of the Home was that inmates were to do nine
months’ work without pay—situations to be sup-
plied at the end of that period. The prospect of
three-quarters of a year’s hard labor “on the
nod ” made accused open her eyes, and, deciding
that she “wouldn’t take any,” she informed the
court that she preferred that they should go ahead
with the sentence. This they did, awarding the
unfortunate one month. One of the leading lights

sweating gabbler.

Feed is becoming so scarce outside the
town that the rabbits have again taken to resi-
dence in Broken Hill. O’ night (late). they can
often be seen scudding about the main streets.
A not unique sight is a half-inebriated citizen
clinging to a lamp-post and striving to assure
himself that the bunny he sees is not of the
whisky variety.
* * * *
On the night of Jan. 8 and morning of
Jan. 9 three-quarters of an inch of rain fell at
Broken Hill in steady, soaking showers ; 24 hours
before this one-quarter inch had fallen. At noon
on Jan. 9—within less than nine hours of the
time the rain had ceased—a blinding dust-storm
was raging. The clouds were so thick that it was
THE DECEASED.
“ Why is that lady dressed in black, mother?"
“ Because, my dear, she [?] a Sister of Charity."
"Oh! is Charity dead?"
impossible to see from one side of the street to
the other. The storm was not confined to the
Hill. Ten miles out, on the plains, where the
rainfall had been still heavier, the dust was
equally thick. A homestead lessee, out repairing
his dam (the intake embankment had been washed
away that morning) had to knock off to escape
suffocation. This is not the first experience of the
kind in the district.
* * * *
One result of Melbourne’s latest gale was
to show that the young trees along St. Kilda-rd.
had been planted without thought for the mor-
row. They were not put far enough into the light
soil, consequently the surface roots have no hold,
and, now that their branches are spreading, a
well-directed gust of wind blows them down to an
angle of 45. Tree-planting, as a rule, shows very
little regard for age developments. In private
gardens one finds saplings placed in positions
which doom them to destruction as nuisances here-
after.

The latest-discovered Yic leper has been
living among his Chow compatriots at Ballarat for
years without being discovered. The disease has
played hideous havoc with his body and has
attacked his throat in such a way that he will
probably choke as the ulcers progress in virulence

The Herald is so moral that when public men
who sin with
Strange women get divorces at seven a.m. sure
It never dreams of saying nasty things ; for, to
begin with,
That doesn’t pay —and then, you see, the Herald
is so pure.
When any play by Oscar Wilde is staged at the
Criterion
The Herald will not mention him : his name it
can’t endure.
It hears he is a satyr, and it knows itself
Hyperion:
The Herald is so moral—the Herald is so
pure.
If the Herald had been there when other Phari-
sees escorted
The woman in adultery to Jesus for a cure
It would have thrown a stone, but it would never
have reported
An incident so shocking to the moral and the
pure.
The Herald’s knee may bow in church : its judg-
ment ne’er consenteth
To the doctrine of salvation for the pauper and
the boor:
The Herald’s God has mercy for the sinner that
repenteth,
But the Herald is more moral—the Herald is
more pure.


Crowned heads of Europe have always
given their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
Paris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been “ Violette Reims” (quite different from
“ Rhine Violet ”), and “Preciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made ; in fact,
they are. known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*

in its fourth thousand, and may be had from Angus
and Robertson, Sydney. Post free 5/6. #
“ The Patents, Trade Marks and Copyright
Laws form one of the most complicated branches
of legal science.” This is true, and it is practi-
cally impossible for any one unacquainted with
their technicalities to draw specifications and
claims, &c., that will stand the test of a law-suit.
Hitherto expert Patent agents have made charges
simply prohibitive to most inventors, but Mr.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., of 39 Castlereagh-
street (next Hotel Australia), Sydney, and Bolton-
street, Newcastle, having established reciprocal
arrangement with leading Patent agents in all the
principal countries in the world is now prepared
to do the work accurately and effectively at rates
that will place a patent within reach of the poorest
inventor. For the sum of £2O (exclusive of cost of
drawings) he will obtain a certificate of Provisional
Protection (in the first instance) for any invention
in the seven Australasian colonies and Great
Britain, and full Patents all over the world at
similarly reasonable rates. Mr. Griffith also
makes a specialty of designing and registering
Trade Marks and Copyrights.*

* * * ■»
Australian lighthouse-keepers, as a rule,
pass terrible times in their hells of monotony.
Cape Otway seems an exception, as one man made
a little fortune at cattle-breeding on the good land
adjacent, whilst most of the others have lived
decently. At Cape Capricorn the land is not re-
productive, but the keepers are. The three
families number 48 souls, and the Government
now provides a State school as a lighthouse
adjunct. The Solitary Island lighters amuse
themselves going to the mainland, etc. At one
Australian coast station one of two men in charge
died suddenly, and the other was left all through
a stormy night staring at the corpse. Three
watchers now man that light.
* * * •
Q. Governor Lamington is trying to work
op his popularity on some of ex-Governor Nor-
man’s lines. He proposes to strike over from
Brisbane, or Toowoomba, to Thargomindah, and
return to Brisbane via Adavale ; then tour all the
North and visit New Guinea.
* * * *
The absence of any sewerage system is bring-
ing its revenge on Brisbane. All the fever-beds in
the local hospital are occupied with typhoid and
scarlet-fever cases. The weather has been un-
seasonably cool, but when the real mugginess of
the tropical month comes, the local undertakers
will be able to buy their wives new bonnets.
* * * *
In N.Q., the other day, a big hotelkeeper
took unto himself a young and pretty second wife.
Before doing so, however, he cleared out every
lodger in the establishment to the number of over
two dozen. Some men evidently do learn a thing
or two from reading the newspaper-reports.

Whilst detectives often re-
cognise ex-gaol-birds by their cat-
like movements, the D.’s them-
selves can be recognised just as
easily by the peculiarly of their
gait, together with their custom
of hunting in couples. The writer,
the other day, though just returned
after five years’ absence from Syd-
ney, singled out a pair of re-
cently-made D.’s by reason of the
ambling goose-step and the im-
mense undentsndings developed
by every police - force fledgling.
When you meet two men together,
each with the same amble and the
same la:ge feet and the same ob-
trusive air of unobtrusiveness,
nobody can help recognising the
typical detect've at a glance. The
air of desperate unobtrusiveness is
the result of previous training in
the uniform force. The ordinary
policeman learns to loom up like a
tower at the street corner, where
he can command a view of his
“bate” either way. Then he is
made a D., and finds that it is his
duty to conceal himself as much as
possible, wherefore he becomes a
large, conspicuous man with a
slink,.and looks as if he was Chang
trying to hide behind himself on
the Old Man Tlain.

Adelaide Hcspital - ities are
fast degenerating. One of the dis-
puting medicoes shaped up to a
rival, the other day, and offered in
a faint voice to punch his head.
Offer declined. This ought to in-
dicate that matters are subsiding.
The average hospital doctor main-
tains his desperate combat with
the bare jaw in the newspapers,
and when he offers, however feebly,
to personally stoush his enemy, it
signifies that his adjectives have
given out.
The cheerful city of Townsville, Q. (where
the thermometer goes about 3° higher at night
than in the day during the summer season), de-
pends largely for its water-supply on two wells
that are alleged to be on lower levels than the
adjacent cemetery.

Miss BEATRICE GRIPFITES.
Photo., The Falk Studios.
The portrait herewith represents Miss
Beatrice Griffiths, one of Kowalski s brightest
pupils, and a lady well-known in Sydney musical
circles. Miss Griffiths is shortly to be tendered a
benefit by her many admirers with a view to
helping her on her way to Europe, where all good
Australians go, there to complete her musical
education. When it is completed the opinion of
those who know her best is that Miss Griffiths
will be one of the cleverest musicians that Aus-
tralia has yet produced, and this country,
considering its youth and other disadvantages,
has not done so badly in that branch of art.
* * * *
la answer to a correspondent’s question as
to the derivation of “robbo” (Sydney slang for
the vehicle ordinarily called a “sulky”) “J.P.”
writes as follows (his explanation looks the most
likely of about 20 which have reached The Bul-
letin) :
“ Four Bob Robbo ” —four shillings Robinson. Robin-
son, who lived in the classic suburb of Waterloo, Sydney
(probably so called because most of the residents have
long since discarded Wellingtons and Bluchers), came
into a bit of money and bought a horse and trap. The
money was spent, and Robinson tired of feeding the
horse, which got poor; so he then sometimes let out the
horse and trap (both somewhat worse of wear) for 4s. per
half-day. There was a run on the cheap hire, and Rob.
bought two other horses and traps, which he let out
at same price. A neighboring livery-stable keeper
and his employes resented Rob’s cutting-down prices,
and, when any of the rival’s equipages passed, used to
cry out, in derision, “ Four Bob Robbo ! ” The cry was
taken up by the kids, and has now become a Waterloo
classic. It has already cost one person ±'2 and costs, the
magistrate ruling that “Four Bob Robbo !’’ shouted after
the original in the street, constituted insulting language
calculated to create a breach of the peace. Verb. sap.
Another correspondent remarks that
If you desire to hear some ornate blasphemy, tell a
Sydney bottle-merchant, that his turn-out is a ‘ four bob.’
You will get four shillingsworth. “Robbo” has in
an extensive Sydney circle come to mean anything un-
satisfactory. For instance, a girl enters a jeweller’s shop
with : “ Whatcher been givin’ us ? Look at the clasp of
this ’ere bracelet I bought of yer last week. It’s gone
bung already. It’s a fair robbo.”
Yerily an illustration of the devious ways of ety-
mology, for few people would hesitate to believe
that “robbo” was a near relation to “robbery.”
Also “robbo” has come to mean amateur : a well-
known amateur cross-country horseman who is a
personal favorite with the professional steeplechase
jocks., when he went out to ride in a ’chase, the
other day,. was amiably greeted with, “ Hello,
Robbo—going ter break yer bloomin’ neck.”
* * * *
Several of the men off the warships now
at Hobart have “ broken leave,” and the usual
“ rewards ” of All, and in one case £3, have been
offered for their arrest. Which recalls a Hobart
incident of some years ago. A “mid” off a war-
ship then in port had broken leave, a reward was
offered for his arrest, and the police were on the
qui vive. One day a boat suddenly shot out
from the wharf and pulled rapidly for the war-
ships. A police-boat was soon in hot chase and
rapidly gained on the waterman and the aforesaid
“mid.” At last the “bobbies” came alongside
and called on the mid. to surrender; but the
mid. suddenly jumped up in the boat and drove
the butt-end of his oar clean through the planking
of the police-boat. Nothing daunted, however,
the police, keeping one of their number baling,
continued the chase and again caught up to the
waterman just as he reached the landing-stage on
the warship : but it was too late —the mid. was on
deck in no time, and a grinning marine with a
fixed bayonet met the eager detective who had
bounded up the ladder. The chase was seen
from the warships and the shore, and the cheering
on both sides was tremendous.
* * * *
All industry of which very little has been
heard so far has just been discovered on a rather
large scale in Melbourne. In that city the man
who wants to pass the Civil Service or any other
examination, and who has doubts about getting
through, hires a person to wear his name for the
time being and pass for him. His double, having
probably passed half-a-dozen times already, can
guarantee to get through, and his charges being
moderate it is a very comforting arrangement for
all parties concerned.
A Bulletin correspondent draws attention
to the hours during' which the so-called “ appren-
tice clerks” in N.S.W. Railway Dept, allegedly
work. Their duties begin at 9 a.m., and they are
permitted to leave when the work is finished—
often as late as 8 and 9 p.m., for which no extra
pay is allowed. Even the much-maligned private
employer will allow “tea-money” for overtime
work, but' the Railway Dept, does not. Eleven
and twelve hours a day is too much to expect
from youths just leaving school.
* * * *
Brisbane (Q.) Society for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Animals, which has just dispensed
with the services of an inspector because, in his
zeal for ferreting out information, he frequently
knocked out himself, considers that its functions
embrace a very wide scope. In its dictionary,
“cruelty to animals” means cruelty to dogs,
horses, children, parents, wives, and. everything
aliv.e. It embraces moral and mental as well as
physical cruelty. Its scope of definition includes
anything that can be squeezed by any artifice
of logic whatever under the all-pervading authority
of the self-appointed Gimlet Society. A hideous
revelation has scummed up to the surface of public
scandal in Brisbane, as part of the bubbling
ferment Caused by the inspector’s resignation. It
is whispered every whei-e that, at the time of that
resignation, no less than 25 names were listed to
certain police authorities as those of persons under
surveillance for suspected incest. .

Hobart rejoices in having the offices of
Police Magistrate and Commissioner of Police and
Commissioner of the Court of Requests combined
in one man. Loud and continuous were the mur-
murings when this little arrangement was made,
but they gradually subsided —till this little inci-
dent again fanned the flame of indignation : On
15th Dec. one of the constables pleaded
guilty to cruelly ill treating a horse. The Police
Magistrate and a J.P. (one of the police commit
tee) fined him ss. with 7s. fid. costs !
The Baldheads
Girl
A hairless tract his scalp displayed
Th at shone so brightly ;
“ Alas ! ” he grumbled to the maid,
“ You deem me ancient, I'm afraid,
And mock me rightly ;
My baldness tells of youth decayed."
His tone teas sad, his bearing staid,
But she laughed lightly.
“ With craft matured I can employ
A tongue beguiling,
And if your mood be chastely coy
I talk of love sans lust alloy —
Parc, undetiling.
But though you feign a sober joy
You can't regard me as your boy."
Hoiv sweet her smiling !
Melb.
She smooths her suitor's time-worn face.
With hands caressing :
“ I'm sick of hoys who ‘ go the pace ; ’
Your heart is young in any case,
And that's a blessing.
A loss of hair is no disgrace ”
Tiptoe upon his shiny place
Her warm mouth pressing.
She pouts the lips that lately kisst
The bald-head gleaming ,
And — well, of course, he can't resist:
Misgivings fade like morning mist
’Neath icarm sunbeaming,
His Ungers clutch her pulsing wrist,
The old man's thoughts can still consist
With love's young dreaming.
EDMUND FISHER.
THE OLD GIRLS.
The old girls are never old ;
Now, who ventures a denial ?
Though their years be all untold,
The old girls are never old ;
Oh, their hair retains its gold,
And their cheeks defy the dial.
The old girls are never old ;
Now, who ventures a denial ?
Sydney.
THOR. D.

The opening, a few weeks hence, of Lever
Bros.’ Sunlight Oil Mills, at Balmain (Sydney),
will put a new cattle-food on the N.S. W. market.
Copra cake, made of the residue of the coc-oanut
after the oil is extracted, is the most nutritious
cattle-food known, being ahead even of linseed
cake, while it is very much cheaper than the latter.
It is reckoned to be times as nutritious as
turnips, and five times as nutritious as fresh grass,
and, as it will be available in large quantities, it
should be of great value, especially in seasons
when feed is scarce.*
