A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Jan. 19, 1897.
My Dear Myee—
The P.A. Yacht Club’s annual festive outbreak
happened on Wednesday evening, when the
steamer Alathea was filled at the Quay by a joyous
crowd, accompanied by a vigorous band and
cheered by thoughts of refreshments. The com-
modore and family were taken on board at Darling
Point in an outbreak of colored fire, rockets, and
fire balloons, and the cornet came out strong in
“ He’s a jolly good fellow.” Club magnates were
identified by their uniform caps and the bronzing
that comes from braving the dangers of the mighty
deep every Saturday half-holiday on the ’arbor. A
concert was given by an amateur tenor, who war-
bled well, a comic singer with a loud, clear voice,
and popular selections were played by the band,
which mercifully omitted “Ben Bolt’’and “Para-
dise Alley,” but couldn’t resist having its fling at
the “ Intermezzo.” Sustenance was handed round
in trays.
Colombo is booming just now as a fashionable
resort with opulent travellers from this side, who
arrange to stop over at the chief hotel in Ceylon’s
Isle on their way ’Ome, and in the time acquire
usefully aristocratic Anglo-Indian acquaintances
whose names they can air when they continue
their voyage, and so paralyse their “colonial”
friends who are ignorant of the social distinctions
of India’s coral strand. A local aspirant to higher
spheres of Niceness is expressing her intention of
exporting herself and grown-up daughters to Cey-
lon for the coming cold weather “because one
meets such Nice People at the hotel there.”
A well-known lady has got herself into quite an
accumulation of worry through good-naturedly
chumming with a clever, and apparently charm-
ing, girl on her way per mail-steamer to visit Aus-
tralia professionally. Having given the actress a
vague invitation to come and see her in Sydney,
she felt no qualms at anything she observed on
board, noting only that her friend had very fine
eyes and a knack of acquiring men friends. But,
after she had been long enough at home to learn
all the current gossip, she heard “things” about
the charmer that made her flee to the uttermost
parts of the mountains to avoid the consequences
of her invitation. Then the actress, after quarrel-
ling with her manager, returned home, and
told startling and original stories to the relations
and friends of the woman who had been too kind-
hearted to snub her, and who had originally been
“ raised ”in the same parts. And amazed rela-
tives write out agonised appeals to know if these
things are true.
The W.C.T.U. is preparing to welcome a new
lecturer from the TJ.S. At a meeting held last
week, the executive committee resolved that a
welcome be “ accorded ” to the enterprising purity
preacher, but cautiously added a rider “ that no
monetary responsibility be undertaken.” His
welcome will probably be endorsed : “Every care
taken but no responsibility.”
A Sydney girl was taken away to Westralia last
week by the marriage of Miss Rosalie Conway to
Mr. Strelitz, who is settled in Fremantle. Another
wedding of the week which takes the bride away
from her native heath was Mr. D. M. Barry, son
of a Melb. gilded citizen, to Miss Frances Tib-
betts, daughter of the popular Gunning doctor.
While a male school inspector was examining
sewing the other day, and trying to look wise
about it, a lady teacher handed up a specimen of
work to the inspector, remarking, “ This is very
poor,” with which dictum the great man agreed
at once. She continued to criticise every piece
till she reached the last, over which she went into
ecstacies —the inspector sharing her raptures. The
teacher had artfully taken up the first garment
once more and palmed it off as a masterpiece of
workmanship.
Bicycles create camaraderie and break down pre-
liminary stiffness, besides flying or wheeling dead
in the face of Mrs. Grundy. Two girls with
bicycles were standing outside Melb. Govt. House
gates waiting for the relay-ride despatch carrier to
arrive, but were in doubt if he would come in by
that gate. One of them suggested consulting the
sentry. “Not much,” remarked the other, “if a
fellow on a cycle comes along I’ll ask him, but I
draw the line at soldiers.’
An enticing placard was shown on hoardings
during the recent festive outbreak. It set forth
the amusements to be derived from a marine ex-
cursion, “the grandest of the season.” The ex-
cursionists had their outing planned for them to
land somewhere down the ’Arbor, spend the day
in “sport, fishing, bathing, fern-gathering,
dancing, &c.” The first-named allurements in the
artistically-worded poster all lead up to the climax.
All the other attractions of the picnic fade before
the entertainment discreetly veiled in the mean-
ingful “&c.” There is generally a great deal of
“ &c.” in every Harbor trip.
Collar-bones are to be covered up with frock in
the near future; the newest ball-frocks are all
creeping up again over the shoulders which were
so long left uncovered. Some croaker has dis-
covered that the relative value of silk or satin as
a surface is greater than that of bone and wrinkle,
to which the stiff, hard, high collars of every-day
wear have reduced the necks of all of us. Some
royal personage had a badly-scarred throat which
she wished to conceal, and so had invented for her
those unyielding buckram gorgets over which
every civilised woman looks and moves stiff-
necked. The lengthy, graceful, swan-like necks
have gone out with the ringlets and fawn-like,
trustful glances of our grandmothers.
The girl who models her fashions on stage
heroines is presenting just now some wildly in-
congruous types of apparel. The enormous Pot-
teresque hat makes every side-walk shady, and
now the plagiarist has to combine that effect with
the hair-dressing of the Chinatown girls, whose
locks appeared to have started in life as a pigtail
which became ambitious of being a knob, and
were checked in mid-career by a ribbon tied in
what sailors call “an Andy Gilligan hitch.” And
she tries to copy Emma Temple’s slim brown
shoes and Ida Hamilton’s elbow-sleeves and glove-
less digits, and if she could only dye her hair the
exact tinge of Harrie Ireland’s “ tresses like the
morn ” she would present an even more con-
glomerate impression than she does now.
Gorgeousness in man just at present is running
to waistcoats. I have seen some really exquisite
vests lately in pale artistic green cloth, with
buttons to match; these are newer than the tan
which, however, still glows on the manly chests of
many of the best-dressed local youths. Summer
ties are formed in bows (I could make a pun here
if I tried), and are a most poetic carelessness in
fluttering ends sometimes. I saw a beauteous
youth loitering in King-street one afternoon. His
clothes were grey, his vest was green, his tie was
cream-and-pink, his boots were tan, his shirt was
limp, the sort that does not shrink; his hat was
soft, with muslin twist, his face was shaven clean,
his stick was hooked, his collar tall, and of such is
the Kingdom of Heaven.
The Artists’ Society has been cheering up the
Eictures in the Temperance Hall, Pitt-street, by
aving weekly afternoon-tea soirees in aid of a
building-fund. Last Saturday’s affair was dis-
tinctly successful—the chairs were all filled with
company listening to music and other adjuncts by
volunteer artists, including a ballad or two by a
fiery-hearted baritone who expressed his longing
to seize his Arab steed by the mane and gallop
over the burning desert, though he looked as if he
never mounted anything more Arabian than a
bicycle.
The dresses in “ True Irish Hearts ” are not so
thrilling as they were said to be in the “special
notice ”on the programme. They are muslin and
many-caped as to the sleeves, and all made after
one pattern with a big flounce round the skirt.
On Monday night a substitute hastily donned the
persecuted S wanton’s gowns and read her part
unconvincingly from the book. Frank M. Clark
wore, for no apparent reason, a tiny shell-jacket
and knickerbockers of brown velvet and looked
like a colossal Little Boy at Manly, and his first-
act boots subsequently walked down the broad
road on the unprincipled, unsteady feet of Scot
Inglis. It is a highly original play as to pronun-
ciation. Chas. M'Carthy, with a voice like the
swish of a shillelagh, talks chestnuts with a brogue,
another character has an unexplained, stage-Ger-
man twang, and the soubrette jokes with difficulty
in Irish until the yacht-scene, where she appa-
rently throws her accent overboard, for it isn’t
very noticeable afterwards. The whole cast stand
by and watch the villain “ aducting ” two children
with whom he plays a game of draughts, removing
first the boy and then the girl, and the good people
lurk in “ruings” and, observing the ruffian’s
mania for burdening himself with puny infants,
remark that they “ sorr ’im steal the kids.” Also,
F. M. Clark kneels on the stage and prays aloud,
which is a most edifying spectacle.
The record price for a drink was paid by a
Westralian prospector, who was found dying of
thirst, in the bush, by a traveller. Too far gone
to speak, the gold-seeker asked by signs for water,
of which the other could give him only half a
pannikin—even then reducing his own chances of
reaching Coolgardie alive :so little had he. The
rescued man’s tongue was black and swollen,
and useless, and he had to write his thanks : “I
have just found a good thing, and, when I sell the
show, I’ll give you half for this.” When the show
was sold, he got .£12,000 for it, and, loyally stick-
ing to his word, gave his rescuer half the profits.
It is a good story, anyhow.
One of the very Nicest matrons in an Austra-
lian capital has a name-double who brings her
into much tribulation by her notorious goings-on.
In one day’s paper the correct and proper Mrs. B.
Dash’s name is recorded as doing good work on
committees and charity-worries, and she sits in the
seats of the mighty, while the next day’s issue
records also that the other Mrs. B. Dash, the local
Lady Munro, has been run in for “ shaking ”
potatoes on the wharf or dangling her black and
rusty bonnet in the faces of the force and “ re-
sisting the police in the execution of their duty.”
The lofty Mrs. D. is too much above suspicion to
necessitate her writing an indignant “Not the
woman” letter signed in full to the news-dis-
seminator, but it gives her pain and sleepless
nights to think that her aristocratic cognomen can
be borne by one of the dregs of creation.
The public ladling-out of grease by the more or
less professional orators who barnacle themselves
on to every notable incident, and the being
labelled “heroes,” was probably the worst of the
ordeals lately undergone by the plucky Orient
boat’s crew whose memorable deed was so enthu-
siastically signalised at Sydney Town Hall on
Tuesday night. lam told that when on the
steamer, the night after the rescue, the passengers
made a presentation to the Orient men, Chief-
officer Ranken, while evidently anxious that his
men’s gameness and endurance should be recog-
nised, hung his head down and squirmed visibly
as his own praises were being sung—he could not
have been less at ease had he been presented at
the police-court on the impossible indictment of
beating a woman. I hear that Mr. Ranken is a
native of Bathurst (N.S.W.) and scion of a well-
known district family—and that he is married to
the daughter of a prominent Sydney accountant.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
A Melb. correspondent to The Bulletin :
The writer is once more confirmed in the opinion that
Melbourne’s most sensational dailj' paper keeps a large
stock of blocks pourtraying imaginary faces. Those or
Brady and his alleged slayer (both well known to the
present scribe) must be purely imaginary, as there is not
the remotest resemblance between the portraits and the
originals. Those, by the way, who know the associations
existing between Brady and Phillips are firmly convinced
that there is a woman in the case. The lad accused of
murdering the book-keeper for years used, it appears, to
write letters for Brady to the woman he had “ in tow,” so
that Brady might avoid possible complications by the
production of letters ; and it is known that more than one
of the dead man’s lady friends were “in trouble.” It is
quite feasible that on Brady’s intended marriage to
another girl becoming known to a deceived one, to whom
he might have promised matrimony also, she might have
taken revenge on him with the assistance of an accom-
plice. Anyhow, young Phillips’ easy demeanour under
circumstances that would try the oldest criminal suggests
that he “ knows something,” and the writer is confident
that sensational developments will take place soon after
this sees print.
“ Maroogaline ” to The Bulletin :
A recent Bulletin paragraph would seem to imply that
Parry-Okeden, the present Q. Commissioner of Police,
went North recently with the intention of whitewashing
the aboriginal contingent of his men-whereas he is a man
who would hang a policeman or a private citizen with
equal celerity if he had evidence that either had killed a
blackfellow without good cause. He is a long-tried friend
of the blacks, was reared among them in Gippsland, where
his father was the first squatter to bring in the wild
tribes and establish friendly relations. Young Okeden
learned their language and the use of the spear and
boomerang, mixed in their corrobborees, and joined them
in their He did the same in Queens-
land, and still speaks fairly well the Wacca and Cabbee
dialects of the Darling Downs and Wide Bay. I have
reason to believe that he intends to abolish the native
police force as organised at present, and establish a new
order of things more creditable to the country and in-
finitely more humane to the aboriginals. He will effectu-
ally abolish the old type of native police-officer, the old
style of dispersal, and place a potent curb on the abo-
riginal-killing species of pioneer settlers.
Dear old £ £ Smith, of the amazing fu r
overcoat, got himself interviewed in Tasmania re'
eently, and poured a cataract of advt. into the
local pressman. A sample :
I ran two newspapers, one in Ballarat and one in Mel-
bourne, the latter called the Times and Mines, and I
called a racehorse after it. I also had the Australian
Journal, and it was in this journal I got Marcus Clarke
to write the celebrated novel, “ His Natural Life.” I have
written about a dozen works on medical subjects. I also
caused to be made, at great expense, an anatomical
museum, at which I lectured, afternoons and evenings, to
crowded houses. I must have had an iron frame, for at
this time I was chairman of many companies, was lectur-
ing twice a day on fresh subjects, supervising two papers,
president of two Royal Commissions ; I had two farms of
my own, one an experimental farm, for the people, on
which I grew cabbages which cost me 9d. each, and I
could buy them in the market at two for Id. ; I was
member of Parliament, and writing my “Almanac” and
also articles on different medical subjects for tlqe Press,
and yet I carried on the largest practice in all the Aus-
tralias, amounting at one time to £13,000 per year. . . .
On my return from Paris I was banqueted by my fellow
citizens, at which the Premier, the Speaker, the leader of
the Opposition, with a large gathering from both Houses,
and the chairmen of different institutions, where i was
again publicly thanked for my humble services, and I
came to Tasmania, and I am fined £lO and costs by a Mr.
Hall, at Zeehan, and you ask me if I have had a pleasant
trip for my holiday 1
A man who saw the Orient-Phyllis affair
writes to The Bulletin :
The relief of the starving seamen of the brig Phyllis by
a boat’s crew from the liner Orient, which formed the
subject of the big public demonstration presided over by
Gov. Hampden in Sydney on Tuesday night, was a truly
soil-ring deed. The big steamer on its way from Mel-
bourne to Sydney encountered a hurricane. It raged
for many hours and raised a mountainous sea, which con-
tinued when the wind abated. In pursuance of mail-
steamer tactics, Oapt. Inskip put out far from land, and
there, remote from the ordinary steamer-track, found the
brig, her sails blown to ribbons, helplessly wallowing in
the trough of the huge waves. Signals showed that her
crew' were starving, and as she had lost her boat,
remained but for the Orient, herself tossed about like
a cork by gigantic short seas which broke every
time, to send a boat with food. It was an appalling task
truly a forlorn hope, but Mr. P. J. Ranken, R.N.R., the
chief-officer, offered to face it, and at once picked a crew
of six from the numerous seamen who volunteered. The
gallant six were not a big set of men, three of them were
distinctly undersized, but they were a well-knit, tough
lot, fit to pull for their lives, which was exactly what
they had to do for the next three hours and a half. Hav-
ing put on life-belts, they got into a clean-lined, smart-
looking boat of semi - whaler tvpe hung near the
steamer’s stern, and took their daring plunge—in which
their craft was so'badly stove that thereafter she had to
be continuously baled. But they got away, and soon it
was perceived that the boat had not only a grand set of
oarsmen but that it possessed in the chief-officer a
rough-water helmsman such as is rarely met except in a
whaling crew 7 . So tremendous were the seas that for
quite half the time you could not catch, even from the
high vantage of the Orient’s hurricane-deck, a single
glimpse of the boat as it chased the distressed bri«- which
rapidly went to leeward, and thus greatly added’ to the
big task the Orient men had set themselves. When the
boat was lowered the brig was within about 500yds. of the
mail-steamer, now hove-to, but owing to the confused
breaking sea and varying wind it is probable that the
crew had to pull six or seven times that distance to reach
the stern of the brig, over which supplies were with diffi-
culty passed by line. With the return began the real
troubles of the Orient men. The sky to windward as-
sumed an evil scowl, along came a hurricane-squall and a
still more difficult sea, and for a time neither brig* nor
boat was visible from the Orient. Gloom settled on the
steamer s crew and passengers, for night was near and for
boat to be separated from steamer in such water meant
certain death for the returning seven—and, at best how
were the men to board the ship ? But the boat at last did
re appear, on the port side, from which it had been
dropped and which had now become the weather side
and when at length it got within 100yds. of the
big vessel the now-exhausted men looked up—as if
to measure their chance-and the people on deck looked
down, far down into the wave-hollow, and thought of
them in the same helpless way one thinks of the drown-
mg dog which he sees vainly treading water along the sea-
wall, devoid of any landing-place. “Buckley’s show”
was plainly the portion of that gallant band of sportsmen
Can tdo it, sir shouted the chief-officer, above the roar
of the storm, to the anxious captain on the bridsre—“ too
dangerous-must try the other side.” The captain told
the mate and his men to save themselves, for the boat
could not be saved. And then another rain-squall came
and in the growing darkness half-an-hour, during which
the boat was sometimes visible and sometimes utterlv in
visible from the ship, was spent in getting to lee Anri
what a lee it was ! When those on deck looked down and
saw in the great gulfs the little boat, on which the electric
light had now been turned, some of them went below that
!u ey , n °t see tlle end. One man, more “done”
than the rest, was pulled up on a bow-line, but the others
simply hung- on to ropes, and-marvellous feat of en
durance under the circumstances-were hauled the whole
way from boat to bulwarks. Three men came up on one
line—the chief-officer was the last man out of the boat
and not one of the lot could stand when he reached
deck. The boat drifted away in the darkness, and has
since been found beached, near Ulladulla. The men, when
they reached the weather-side of the steamer, certainly
never expected to see land again, and the chief-officer
one of them told the present writer next morning said
time for prayers had come.” Altogether, it was a si-ht
to make a man s blood tingle, but it was one which no
body would care to see twice.

* * * *
Auckland R.C. has at last resolved to bracket,
for betting purposes, all horses similarly owned in the
same race. The defeat of the G.N. Stakes favorite,
Multiform, by his stable companion, Medallist, is respon-
sible ; yet the main result in such cases may be that both
horses will start at a short price, instead of one a hot
favorite and any odds the other.

careful in picking his words re Tat.tersall Adams. He is
opposed to the sweeps, being in the Opposition, and at
the same time he is a candidate for Brighton electorate ;
and to the Brighton Racing Club, which Henry D. patro-
nises, Adams has given £5O. Ergo, H.D. had, on New
Year’s Day, to “ cordially express his gratitude” to Mr.
Adams for his “ munificent gift,” and at the same time to
say no word in favor of his being settled in ’Obart.

“ Could George Adams,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “ explain how it is that horse-owners get
to know the names and addresses of ticket-holders in
sweeps? A friend of the writer’s received a wire from a
horse-owner in a recent race two days before the list of
Tatt.’s drawing came to hand, when he knew his number
represented •. Of course he ‘ arranged ’ matters
with the owner ; but to make up he plunged all his spare
cash on the horse at 25 to 1, and, as the animal got home,
he managed to get a bit even with the man who
threatened to ‘ scratch ’ if his terms were not agreed to.”

Artist Carrington and his wife, both of the
Australasian, are reported to have given Ikey Mo quite
a hard knock, at the races lately. The press so seldom
gets a reasonable revenge upon its hook-nosed acquaint-
ance that The Bulletin records this startling rumor with
much pleasure, and hopes that the happy pair hacked the
double, or whatever it was, for nothing’ less than half a
million.

Tom Lees and the one-time sensational horse
Torpedo, disqualified in Australia, are still companions in
South Africa, where Lees still knocks a race and a few
pounds out of the old ’un. How time flies ! Ten years
last. September since Peter Jackson and Tom Lees met at
Foley’s old White Horse for £4OO and the championship,
and Jackson chased Lees round the ring for two solid
hours before cornering him.
* * * *
A Melb. paper, commenting on the attempt to
revive trotting handicaps at Canterbury Park, says : “ No
doubt this step, if adopted by other clubs and regularly
pursued by Canterbury, will attract some of the trotting
identities from this colony, which will be a misfortune foi
Sydney.” All over the world the same cry. Trotting
races breed more dangerous blackguardism than the other
kind of horse sport. The ill-fated boy who fancies pigeons
is father to the flash person who loves a fast trotter.
* * * *
For the trot at Brighton, Sydney, on Monday,
Harry Knight, driving Edith, led into the straight, but
Gus Milson had a bit left in Creole, and taking his only
chance brought him up on the inside. Knight was quite
in his province had he closed in on the rails, but he drew
out and gave a clear field. The pair then trotted nose-
and-nose to the finish, no one being able to divide them,
until the judge declared for Creole by a nose. This is the
sort of finish trotting wants more of to take on with the
crowd.

Premier Nelson is the least-known public
man in Brisbane. It is quite an event to see him
in Queen-street. When not at the Treasury count-
ing che public debt he is at Toowoomba or South-
port.
The Mayor of an Australian capital is so
involved that it is not improbable he will be in the
Insolvency Court before the completion of his
term of office. Moneyed men are making a set
against him for political reasons.
Melbourne boasts of at least a score of
lady-detectives, several of whom move in the best
society. One, well-known to the writer, earned
over <£4oo last year, and still manages to hide her
profession from her most intimate friends.

tralian mine lately wrote to the London directors
that he would have to put down a shaft which
would cost <£2500. Whereupon a director said :
*' Can’t we get a second-hand one and send it out?”

of knowledge in a function speech to a lot of

It is printed that Queen Victoria is now a
teetotaller ; three years ago she entirely aban-
doned alcohol, and is quite satisfied with the ex-
periment. It can be said for Queen Victoria,
anyhow, that on this subject she is not bigoted,
having given both sides a fair trial.
The name Phillips will presently be in as
bad odor as that of Butler. One Phillips is
charged at Melbourne with the murder of book-
keeper Brady, and two of the same name, aged 16
and 14 years respectively, are committed for trial
at Mudgee for murdering their father.

Re that “Life of Parkes,” author Charley
Lyne can’t well be blamed for dealing with his old
patron in a friendly, not to say generous, spirit.
The deceased ex-Premier was an excellent friend
to Mr. Lyne, whom he took from the drudgery of
press-work and made Secretary of N.S.W. Public
Works Committee at ,£7OO a year.
It is recorded of one Australian knight
that he more or less earned his bauble by giving
the Governor of the day some valuable advice
respecting a “ really good thing ” into which the
crowd who had induced Lord Carnarvon and
others to speculate in city property sought to put
His Excellency.
Robert Porteous, who had been confined in
the debtors’ side of Darlinghurst Gaol since March,
1892, for non-payment of £99, due as alimony
to his wife, was lately released through the action
of the N.S.W. Minister for Justice. Porteous
could have obtained his release after 12 months’
confinement by filing his schedule in bankruptcy.
This he refused to do. Under an Act passed in
1896 the Minister can assume the responsibility of
filing the schedule whether the debtor likes it or
not. This Act cuts two ways—it releases the
debtor, and, possibly, obtains from his estate (if
he has one) the required alimony.
The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent writes : “A man of high
professional standing told me this
strange tale, the truth of which he
vouches for. There were two students
attending Glasgow University. One
was English and the other American.
They were inseparable chums, and
painted the ancient city of St. Mungo
bright red on many occasions. Then
they were jointly seized with a gloomy
and awful variety of religion, and swore
off wassail and festivity. Later, the
American set out with his mother and
sister for a season in Paris. They were
very wealthy, and the women-folk ex-
cessively fond of frivolity, but the son’s
drab-coiored creed was to them a wet
blanket that twines round the neck and
drips down the spine. He sat heavily
on their joys and talked to them in a
nasal monotone about their souls—until
he fell bodily ill. He grew worse, and
suddenly better ;much worse, and again
much better. The doctors were puzzled
completely. He died midst the love
and lamentations of his broken-hearted
relatives—and there is now little doubt
that it was a ease of systematic poison-
ing. To-day the sister resides in
Wormwood Scrubs Prison, and is
known to the world as that ‘ much-
wronged woman ’ Mrs. Maybrick,
doing a life sentence for the alleged
murder of her husband. My informant
says he was the other student. He
has lost his drab-colored creed and
takes a moderate amount of wassail in
bis respectable middle age, and he isn’t
one of the Maybrick band of sympa-
thisers —not by any means.”
Father Fouhy, of Brisbane (Q.), when in-
terviewed by a Denver (U.S.A.) newspaper-man,
during his recent “grand tower,” was asked :
“ What is your name ? ” “Oh, put me down as
the Queen of Sheba.” “What have you to say
about anything?” “Nothing. Good-night.”
And the Denver man absquatulated and fabri-
cated a column and a-half of a most interesting
interview.
Henry Wenden, another of the men who
narrowly escaped going on a death-trip with
Butler, says that Butler “ appeared to be a most
pleasant fellow, and had a very taking manner.”
Very taking. Very undertaking also. But really
this al fresco murderer must have possessed much
personal magnetism. If he had devoted his atten-
tion to politics instead of murder he might have
done better—perhaps.
Ash, Premier Kingston’s law-partner,
lately sought to deduct the price of his wig (£8),
tram-fares between his office and home, and the
cost of a law newspaper from the taxable amount
of his income, but the magistrate would not allow
more than the newspaper. There is to be an
appeal to the Supreme Court, and Ash, a very
stubborn personage, threatens to go thence to the
Privy Council if he does not get what he regards
as justice.
The case of Mrs. Cecile Anderson, found
guilty, in Melbourne, some years ago, of shooting
one Fraser, under very extenuating circumstances,
is brought up now and then. But, somehow, her
episode never seems to get a hold on the public
mind, though the public raves a good deal over
many less deserving. She is gaoled and forgotten.
There was a very considerable-sized doubt in Mrs.
Anderson’s case all along, and a very little differ-
ence in the jury’s liver should have saved her.
She and Fraser were in a room together, and,
when the noise of some considerable shooting
attracted attention, one was found dead and the
other injured. The lady said that he shot her
unsuccessfully, and then shot himself successfully.
The jury decided, for no very observable reason, that
she shot him successfully, and then went gunning
for her own brains, and made a failure of it.
Probably the hair that turned the scale was the
fact that Fraser had treated Mrs. Anderson so
badly that any woman in her place would natu-
rally feel like shooting him ; and this fact might
as well have been read the other way about—
Fraser’s conduct towards Mrs. Anderson showed
him to be bad enough to shoot her or anybody
else. The case was more-or-less of a toss-up, and
it isn’t at all certain, even yet, whether the coin
came down endways or not.
SOMETHING WRONG WITH THE
SOAR.
“ Yaas, I was washed at the fount, and am
whiter than snow! ”
Geo. Thorn and Wm. Thorn are brothers
and Q’land M.L.A.’s. George is fat and jolly,
Bill is lean and sorrowful. George warns the
Government of impending dooin, and always sits
and votes with them. William is one of the
“ remnant,” or Opposition. He occupies his time
in the House either in denouncing the bicycle on
the ground that it is ruining the farmers, or in
advocating the Goombungee railway. No one
else knows where Goombungee is. In division
the brothers invariably face each other.
Ecclesiastical affairs are somewhat mixed
in Brisbane. Rev. G. D. Buchanan is leaving the
pulpit to be a Government immigration lecturer.
Smith, the man who takes a deep interest in the
local aboriginal, has retired from the vineyard
through old age, and the Scots Church meenister,
Rev. J. F. M‘Swaine, is in London where he has
had 19 operations performed on his throat, without
success. Also, a local reverend is leaving owing
to ; at least people say it is owing to ,
but the matter has been keot very secret.

Lately-deceased Gov. Weld placed the
Westralian press “on a proper social basis.” In
the early days many members of the “ Fourth
Estate ” were “old-timers,” and so were not
actually admitted to public feeds, but were allowed
to come in afterwards and report the speeches.
One “ untainted ” scribe demurred to this arrange-
ment, and refused to take any note of the
speeches at a public function. His refusal and the
reason of it coming to the ears of the then
Governor, orders were given that at any social
function at which H.E. was present the reporters
were to be admitted on the footing' of ordinary
guests. A memorable trouble of like character
occurred at Sydney during the Loftus regime.
When the eminent Arthur Orton was
butchering at Wagga, he was much annoyed by
the local cockatoo farmers stealing meat out of the
slaughter-yards. So one day, when a two-year-
old colt died, he just skinned and dressed the
carcase and left it on the gallows. Next morning
the carcase was gone, and several of the residents
were so queer, they thought they’d die. Arthur
thought “ they must be took bad from eating too
much veal with shoes on.”
Charles Eckenstein, the gilt-edged cycling
“ professor, ” who was allowed to keep his name
dark when giving evidence against a Melbourne
pub. the other week, has now published his
identity by committing suicide. A lady in the
case, perhaps. A man who teaches sassiety to
perform on the giddy bike is pretty sure to fall
madly in love with the first fair pupil who doesn’t
smile upon him. The phenomenal circumstance
is too much for his feelings.
A soft-hatted individual entered Parlia-
ment House, Brisbane, one morning lately, and
said to a messenger: “I’ve an appointment at
this hour with Mr. Nelson.” “Who?” “Mr.
Nelson.” “Who-o-o?” “ Nelson, the Premier.”
“Do you mean Sir ’Ugh ? ” “That’s the bloke."
The messenger staggered. But the interviewer
had merely forgotten the title, and was unused
to using'it. . . . “I found out afterwards,”
said the aghast flunkey, “that he was only a
common newspaper man from down South.”
The talismanic Hugh Williams cannot be
altogether appropriated by the British Admiralty
as its very own. Australia also has its record. At
the big Redfern-railway smash two years ago,
when an incoming suburban and an out going
country train collided, one engineer-driver was
killed and the other escaped absolutely unhurt.
His name was Hugh Williams, and he is at present
employed on the South African railways. Visitors
to the Hawkesbury must be familiar with the
initials, “ H.W.,” printed with charcoal high up
on one of the cliffs on the southern side of the
river midway between the bridge and Kangatoo
Point. They were put there by an adventuresome
Hugh Williams, who, when he had climbed up,
found it was impossible to get back, and from that
height made a big leap out into the river. He was
rescued in an almost drowning state by Captain
Melvey, of the Binghie. When coming off the
Binghie at Brooklyn, he fell into the water, and
was fished out by a small boat alongside. In the
train he had a row with a fellow passenger, and
was thrown out. The guard saw the accident;
train was stopped, and H. W. was picked up none
the worse for his mishap. He had fallen on part
of an old orchard. A Hugh Williams, residing
in Maori]and, was once the winner of Tattersall’s
big sweep. The sole survivor of a memorable
yacht accident on the Yarra in Jubilee year was
Hugh Williams. And early in ’92 the Westralian
papers gave particulars of the finding of two pros-
pectors on the road to Menzies in a terribly
famished and dying condition. One afterwards
succumbed, the other—who pulled through, and is
now in a good way of .business in Perth —was Hugh
Williams.

£ £ Smith’s daddy, showman E. T. Smith
of Drury Lane, founded his fortunes by borrowing
from money-lender Geneste a <£looo-note at £1
per day interest. Armed with this he would
attend auctions, and when the time came for
payment would calmly tender the JROOO-note, for
which the clerk never had change. Awe-inspired
auctioneers would send round the purchases to
Smith’s rooms, and he would re-sell them at a
higher figure and pay up a day or two later. In
a couple of months the note was returned to
Geneste unchanged.

CHAFFING A CONSTABLE. _ J
On the 2nd March last Constable Maxwell/
in tpufti, went to the Port Melbourne pier,
where he attempted to catch fish. A man I
named -Daniel Griffiths saw him, and muttered !
something about a fool and a worm ; and when ;
Maxwell requested him to “keep a civil tongue
in his head,” he used “language.” Maxwell
promptly arrested him and was taking him to
the watch house when he escaped. Yesterday,
coming back from the country, Griffiths showed
himself on the pier, and was'immediately seized
upon by Senior-constable Porter and conveyed
before the local justices, who fined him 20s. ut
three days.

THE CHURCH AND THE FEDERAL CONVENTION.
“The first effeot of Cardinal Moran's candidature will be to transform the battle for Federation into a bitter sectarian struggle, whose end one cannot foresee. If the Cardinal comes out, then members of other Churches will follow, not because they want to get into the
Federation Convention, but as a protest against what they will call clerical domination.”— DAILY PAPER.

Bright’s Disease not Curable.
An impression exists in the minds of many per
sons that Bright’s Disease is incurable. Th<
means frequently resorted to are rarely successful
but when Warner’s Safe Cure is employed, a com
plete cure follows, as instanced by the experi
ence of Mr. Peter Woods, of Woods’ Hotel
corner of Spencer and Bourke Sts., Melbourne
who, in a letter dated. Oct. 20, 1896, writes : I
was cured from Bright’s disease of the kidneys by
the use of Warner’s Safe Cure, and as this was
some three or four years ago, and I have enjoyed
good health since that time, I do not hesitate in
saying the cure was permanent.” *

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Sydney papers have the carriage of
their opinions—gratis.
H. : “The Colonial Office ignores, or
rather is ignorant of, the real power in Victorian
statesmanship. Why not Sir David Syme ? ”
N.S.W. Opposition has voted that Federa-
tion should be treated in a national spirit. The
national spirit is all right, provided the party
hasn’t mislaid the corkscrew.
Owing to the opposition of the Govt, and
the Labor-party in the S.A. Assembly, the land-
grant transcontinental railway to Port Darwin
has been defeated for the present year. A still
more determined effort in its favor is to be made
next session.
One Hobart view of the Hare system : “If
I can’t plump for Mr. B. now, I won’t vote at all.
He’s in my church and I want to plump for him.
If he don’t get in, I’ve got the satisfaction of
knowing I did plump for him, anyhow.” How
very Tasmanian !
Four Q. Labor-members have been made
Jaypees. Last year the Q. Labor-members fussed
largely against any such prospect, but this year
they have quietly swallowed the indignity. No
valid reason why they shouldn’t. It’s a mere
matter of taste. Nevertheless, it shows that the
Nelson crowd knows how to lime the twigs.
There is calm impudence about the decision
of the S.A. Opposition that the Federal Conven-
tion elections should not be run on party lines.
Of the 125,000 male and female voters in the pro-
vince, not m,ore than the odd 25,000 would vote
for the Opposition candidates if a partisan spirit,
as Kingston desires in the Democratic interest,
pervades the contests.
The fiscal issue absolutely can’t help crawl-
ing in at the Federal Convention. Federation
obviously means Freetrade in Australia and Pro-
tection against the world, and it will take the
smartest and least scrupulous Freetrader all his
time to dodge the question already in the mouths
of election barrackers —which probably accounts
for the supineness of Premier Reid when he seems
to have Australia by the ears.

Sydney D.T. and N.S.W. Premier Reid
are now of one mind on the character of Upper
House reform—nomineeism, with rotatory retire-
ments. In Parkes’s day of power that paper was
also of one mind with him on the same subject—
“ the true democratic principle of an elective
Chamber.”
Rockhampton (C.Q.) Record’s retort on
the Brisbane press attitude :
In Southern Queensland th,e objection has been raised
that the manufacturing industries of Queensland would
be injured by Federation. The metropolitan brewers, it
is declared, would all be ruined, as Sydney and Mel-
bourne, with their financial resources and big popula-
tions, would be able to flood Queensland with their surplus
at a price that would knock the local beer out of the
market. At the same time, it is admitted that Brisbane
brewers are doing with respect to Rockhampton and
Townsville exactly what they dread would be done by
Sydney under Federation.
An interesting squabble, if only from the view-
point that Beer as well as Black Man is a “ lion
in the path ” of Q.’s joining the Federation.
An inspired paragraph says that Col. Sec.
Brunker will be one of the Federal Convention
nominees of N.S.W. Ministry. What has Mr.
Brunker done that he should be delegated to help
in framing a constitution for Australia ? Mr.
Brunker is a good-natured, respectable, privately-
honest old gentleman who has auctioned more
bullocks than most men ; he is fat and placid and
writes that copy-book-like hand which was never
the attribute of anyone nearer to genius than an
average tailor’s foreman. But how he ever be-
came N.S.W. Colonial Secretary, or why he
should be set up as a Federal Convention delegate
is a mystery as deep as why Gag Gould should be
continually chosen as N.S.W. Minister of Justice.
Nothing that has previously happened has
done so much to make S.A. look small and mean
and hopelessly parochial as the Adelaide Hospital
dispute. If the fact ever really soaks into the
comprehension of people outside that S. A. politics
have been convulsed for two years over the
appointment of one hospital nurse, and Royal
Commissions have wrestled with the matter, and
it was believed by many that the Kingston
Government would ultimately fall by reason of
it,' then it will be a very long time before S.A.
will again be regarded as a serious institution.
When 903,000 square miles of territory are
roused to excitement for 24 months over the
appointment of one lady at a small salary to be
supervisor of broken legs in an hospital it is diffi-
cult to take that place seriously.
Since Queensland shuffled out of the
Federation movement its politicians have manifes-
ted an unexpected interest in the old, useless
Federal Council —the tired thing that meets at
Hobart once in two years and does nothing. At-
torney-General Byrnes, in particular, wants all
the provinces to pass an Act making the members
of the Federal Council elective, and giving them
enlarged powers ; and he professed to be hurt and
surprised that nobody takes any notice of the
little red herring he is drawing across the track.
Yet Attorney-General Byrnes himself doesn’t
waste his time now patching the knickerbockers
he wore when he was a boy, for the sufficient
reason that he doesn’t want them any more. And
his proposal that Australia, just when it is getting
measured for its new pants, should waste time re-
pairing the pants it wore out the year before last
is sheer stupidity.
A DIFFERENT THING.
NEW-CHUM (“all broke-up”) : “ You said this horse was that quiet a child could
PLAY with him. Just look at me!”
THE DEALER: “That’s true. But you were riding him!”
According to a recent cable, all the British
thread-making companies have amalgamated into
one huge concern with 74 millions capital, and an
attempt is being made to include the American
thread-factories in the combination. If they can
be included the ring will boss the world’s trade,
and fix prices very much as it pleases. The
Foreign-trade theory that rings and trusts only
grow under Protection seems to have gone wrong
just here.
A wail fromLißEßTY —organ of the National
Ass :
Under the Victorian Usury Limitation Bill, no person
may borrow money, and pa}- a greater interest than 8 per
cent. If his security is such that he could only get money
he wanted at, say, 9 or 10 percent., he cannot borrow, and
would, perhaps, have to go to the wall. Heaven save us
from this grandmotherly legislation !
Likewise, in other times and places, a man who
had no other security could borrow on his family
—it being sold into slavery to recover the debt, if
he didn’t pay up. But grandmotherly legislation
has prohibited that in most countries now, and the
man who has no other security to borrow on than
his family has to go to the wall. Yet the wall
seems none the worse, and there are no trade con-
vulsions through a person being prohibited from
raising money on his offspring, and nothing special
has happened.
British Cabinet-Minister Balfour : “If
Ireland were to abandon the manufacture of
whisky, the present grievance ” —respecting alleged
unjust taxation of Ireland by Bull —“would dis-
appear.” Gurroo ! If Ireland abandoned the
manufacture of whisky, Irishmen would disappear.
If the world curled away in a bonfire, Balfour
would disappear. The larger contains the less of
its own kind. Ireland is whisky and whisky is
Ireland —more or less.

“ By the creation of the Federal Parliament as

Maoriland Government intends making a
vigorous effort to carry its Old Age Pension Bill
this year. As a preliminary it contrived last ses-
sion to crowd through a little Act for the registra-
tion and sorting out of all claims on the proposed
pension fund, and the work is now beginning in
earnest. This is at least a proof that business is
intended.
Vic. Freetrader Boyd and the Vic. Free'
trade clan in general ask, scornfully, how Tren-
with can advocate a protective duty on coal
imported into Victoria, and also support Federa-
tion and the consequent abolition of interprovin-
cial Protection ? Another question, equally to the
point, is how Vic. Freetraders concur to this day
in supporting a purely local military force which
is kept ready for use against all comers, the people
of adjacent provinces included, while they pro-
fess to preach unity? What is the exact dif-
ference between maintaining a provincial tariff in
the present while advocating an arrangement
which will make it unnecessary in the future, and
maintaining a provincial military force now while
barracking for a system which will involve its
abolition ? Will the Argus explain ?
If Card. Moran, the big Catholic, is per-
mitted to become a candidate for the Federal
Convention, some little Catholics will naturally
try to crowd in under his wing. First result:
formation of a Catholic “ bunch ” of candi-
dates—certain of election by the block Catholic
vote rounded up by the parish priests from the
pulpit and privately. Second result: formation
of a rival Protestant “bunch,” which, though it
may not be so successful, will be successful
enougti—with the aid of the Freemasons and the
Boyne-water bhoys—to get good representation on
the Convention. Third result: Orange cursing
Green across the floor of the Convention, and
Green yelling for the blood of Orange, and the
business of founding an Australian nation damned
utterly in the strife of the worst sectarian passions.
From all which, Good Lord, and the good sense
of the community, deliver us.
Q. Attorney - General Byrnes (re the
Federal Council): “I have not the slightest dis-
position to be a member of a body that exists on
sufferance, as it were.” (Because Federal Coun-
cillors are not elected by the people, he further
explained. Which was surely very calm on the
part of one of the high priests of bulcockery—
representative of a gang which, but for its roll-
faking and plural-vote insistence, would be in a
hopeless electoral minority.) And wouldn’t
“Tommy” Byrnes have proudly attended the
Federation Convention as a delegate not elected
by the people? Smile right here. For is he not
the quondam “Irish Home Rule” man who
patronised Michael Davitt, and yet who, to retain
his office as Attorney-General, countenanced and
clinched the iniquitous Coercion Act in Queens-
land (Home Secretary Tozer said he was the real
author of it) ? And if “ Tommy ” had popped up
or were to pop up by any future fluke, as a Feder-
ation Convention delegate, would he not be such
“only on sufferance, as it were?” And isn’t
“Tommy” at present an M.P. “only on suffer-
ance, as it were,” by the hustings retirement of
Arthur Morgan at Warwick, and by the grace
(and grease for the R.C. vote) of Premier Nelson
“ as it were ”? ’

There are se-

in connection
with the N.S.W,
Landlord and

about which the
public have
wrong ideas. It
is commonly sup-

a tenant wishes

that period for-

nothing in the

having risen to 92,005 at the end of last year. The
Land of Thrift.
Government by Contradiction places men
over departments of which they know nothing.
Sheridan, unable to work a sum in long division,
became Chancellor of the British Exchequer;
news-vendor Smith, who never went to sea, became
boss of the British navy ; and the untutored Jacob
Garrard controls N.S.W. University and schools !
When Randolph Churchill became Chancellor of
the Exchequer he could not understand a return
until the clerk explained that it was carried out to
decimals. “Oh ! ” exclaimed Churchill, “ I never
knew before what those damned dots meant.”

lin ” cure for the removal of old-standing cases of
piles, without operation, sent to any colony.
Australian Depdt, 257 George-street, Sydney.
Mr. Greenfield, of the well-known firm of
Greenfield and Barraclough, whose eyesight-test-
ing invention is the latest boon to sufferers from
defective vision, is now doing his regular tour of
all the large N.S.W. towns.*
T. H. Molesworth, formerly of the
Adelaide School of Mines has removed his Assay
Office and Chemical Laboratory from 56 Margaret-
street to 5 Moore-street, Sydney, and is open to
do business with all Australasia.*
Wm. Mackenzie, over 30 years manager of
the celebrated Bebeah (N.S.W.) vineyard, has
opened a Sydney depdt for sale of its wines at 379
George-street, Sydney. The Bebeah vineyard has
won 466 first and champion prizes, and its pro-
ducts can be warmly recommended to everybody
who has sense enough to know- that a good, pure
wine at a reasonable price is the most natural and
palatable drink for Australians.*
The Red Bird bicycle, with Bilmartin and
flyer Kellow on top, is riding its way into a good
big business. The Red Bird, with its distinctive
name, has loosened up the Australian bike-trade
tremendously, and A. J. Knowles (“ Hullo Bill ”)
will see that no one gets the jump on him. Bil-
martin’s Red Bird weighs an ounce or two over
18ib., and many people thought that the strong,
heavy plugger would plug the light machine into
dismemberment and dissolution. But William
and his bike are both going strong and well—a
good cyclist and a good cycle.*
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inveptors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough. *

Of late years the Tasmanian summer meetings
nave proved happy hunting-grounds for Ikey Mo and co.,
who have popped over from Vic. to rake in the islanders’
shekels; but all that has been put an end to by the
introduction of the totalisator, no fewer than seven
machines being prepared for the ensuing Cup meeting.
If the “blind” tote proves a success, its early introduc-
tion into Vic. and N.S.W. may be depended upon, a con-
summation devoutly to be wished.
* « * *
The progeny of that sterling stayer, Trident
Robinson Crusoe—Cocoanut), are repeatedly being re-
turned winners. On Saturday last two of his daughters
won at Canterbury Park, N.S.W.; and yet but few owners
of really first-class mares avail themselves of his services.
• * » *
At Canterbury Park, N.S.W., on Saturday last,
Moran weighed out for The King in Flying Welter, but
that horse was subsequently scratched a few minutes
after the authorised time, and yet, under Tattersall’s Club
rule, backers have to pay, the horse being considered a
starter. At Rosehill, only recently, backers had similarly
to pay over Woodford, although that horse was never at
the starting-post, his jockey having taken suddenly ill.
The rule, for the protection of backers, needs immediate
revision, and no horse should lie deemed a starter until it
has come under the starter’s orders. This rule at present
obtains both in Vic. and Great Britain.
* * * *
The anniversary meeting of that somewhat
mysterious body, the Sydney Turf Club, begins to-day
(Saturday) at Randivick. In the Challenge Stakes, the
handicapper has apparently treated Moorefield and Wha-
kawatea as “ has beens,” with the probable result that
one of the pair will be returned winner, Akarini and
Aurea perhaps being their most dangerous opponents.
The Anniversary Handicap, to be run on Tuesday, should
bring out a good field, and the M.L. Sabretache appears
to have a real good chance of winning, and thus proving
that his Derby trial was correct. Glendennon and Kalla-
tina may also run well.
* « * *
Ascot (Vic.) R.C’s Kensington handicap last
week resulted in two dead-heats between Audacity and
The Gift, the latter winning right out in a third go.
* » » »
1897 Ascot (unregistered) Gold Cup lOOOsovs.
will be the biggest stake of its sort in Australia.
• * * *
The handicap for the L.V.W. Cup last Saturday
was an amusing satire on last year’s expedition of Aus-
tralian bike champions to Hingland. Ten scratch men
were placed 20 yards in front of Megson, R. W. Lewis, and
Payne, who were estimated at 10 yards better than ex-
hero Parsons.
* * * *
A summons case in Melb. District Court last
week was calculated to disillusionise the girls who dote
on the dark-eved Italiarii bikists. An ex-cook, bearing the
brigandish name of Bellotti, sued Joseph Gardano, other-
wise known as Boidi, for wages due under an alleged
agreement. Boidi persuaded, or rather encouraged, his
ambitious compatriot, the cook, to abandon the kitchen
for a cycling career, and, for awhile, the aspiring novice
made himself useful in Boidi’s little bike shop. He re-
ceived his food, and half-a-crown per week for Sunday
revellings, also an old bicycle costume, and a vague
promise that he should be educated in the art of winning
a beginners’ race. It transpired in evidence that Porta
asked Bellotti in the shop why he stayed there for a
weekly two and sixpence instead of going back to the
cooking. Probably the romantic foreigner has returned
to the manufacture of gravy by this time. He lost his case
on the ground that he had no agreement with Boidi, but
merely came in the hope of being converted into a
“ champion.”
* * * *
“Just as over-indulgence results in tiredness
and lameness of the leg muscles, so also are the heart and
respiratory muscles affected. The heart, through in-
creased work put upon it by long, rapid spins, is taxed to
its utmost, and, when persevered in, serious damage to
the heart valves may result. Endurance in prolonged
contests is measured by the capabilities of the heart, and
its essence consists in being able to meet the continuous
tendency to overstep the limits of dilatation.” — Professor
Osier, M.D.
* » * *
At a cycle meeting held in a Vic. country town
lately the father of one of the competitors in the prin-
cipal race acted as handicapper. And yet his son only
came in second ! A father who can’t help his own off-
spring better than that ought to stay at home. The judge,
however, made up for pa’s blunder by ruling that the
young man won the race. So says Mr. Rice, a member of
the council of the League of Vic. Wheelmen.
• * * *
It may be almost taken for granted in Aus-
tralian bike-racing' that the winner of a championship
trophy is not a champion.
♦ ♦ * *
That ambulance-stretcher at the L.V.W. meet-
ing last Saturday struck one as being brought in chiefly
for theatrical effect. Falls upon the damp, level turf were
not likely to do much damage, and, as a matter of fact,
none of the men who came down could find an excuse for
pretending that they needed to be borne off the track.
But the tragic suggestiveness of the stretcher probably
served a business purpose, especially among female spec-
tators.
« * * *
London Tailor moans plaintively that cycling
tends to lessen the demand for expensive clothes. Men
who were formerly the slaves of Snip now go around all
day in a knicker suit of tweeds, and—says the afflicted
orvan—“ London would be unbearable and uninhabitable
if morning and frock-coats disappeared and tweed were
installed in their place.” That’s true. And all the poetry
would go out of cockney life if the dock-laborers and
other wealthy classes were to abandon the habit of wear-
ing thirty-shilling silk hats. Also the national necessity
for patent-leather boots has to be considered.
* * * *
At the wind-up of a recently-gone-bung N.S.W.
social-and-recreation club, three members, solid bowling
drinkers, had £7O at their debit “ on the slate” for drinks.

When all their bonnets filled with bees,
They mostly go across the foam,
And, as each local watcher sees,
They get infected by the breeze,
And find “ where drunken rollers comb” ;

* * * *
Mick Dooley wants to meet Joe Goddard once
again. If the pair come together and Michael can be got
into anything like condition, he should win. Joseph
appears to have lost the “ devil ” which characterised his
fighting of old, and it was only Goddard’s whirlwind
rushes and iron determination which roughed Dooley out
of it on both their previous meetings.
Bill Slavm is acting as sparring partner with
Peter Jackson in England, and is said to make a good
showing. When in Australia, William was, without excep-
tion, the poorest boxer to be found about Sydney, and
it sounds like a fairy tale to hear that he has improved to
the extent of making a show set-to with Peter.
* * * *
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League will make its best-
planned effort on March 13 and 20 to attract and hold the
interest of the Sydney public ; and all the League can do
between those dates and the present is to pray for sun-
shine. Prizes total £4OO, and the racing, from the
nature of the programme and from the number and
calibre of the competitors, must rival the Zimmerman
carnival for interest. The Zimmerman carnival had the
great support of novelty and a world-admired star. The
forthcoming carnival will have a splendid novelty in its
one-lap scratch races. There are so many men in Aus-
tralia who think they can lick creation for a lap, and,
therefore, in the great majority of the lap races the sprint
will start from pistol-crack. And in the few races where
the sprint may not start with the pistol, the riders can
only crawl for a few seconds. In long races such as 25 miles
or 984 laps, the competitors can crawl for hours, and the
spectators can be bored to death. The longest race in
the forthcoming carnival will be five miles scratch. And
this will be worth going miles to see, partly because the
pacing will be done by singles, so as to give each rider as
good pacing as another, and chiefly because Bilmartin
will wrestle with all the crack new-comers.
* * * *
Many who have recently bought second-hand
bicycles now anathematise their haste. Six or eight
months ago a “ wheel” in fair condition would sell easily
at anything up to £l3. Several substantial machines are
now on the market, new, at £l2 10s. and upward, and
there is virtually no market for the second-hander.
CRICKET.—THE “ NO-BALL ” GAME.
What seems the same is not the same
Within this world of sin ;
Victoria won a no-ball game,
But not a noble win.
* * * *
When the Australian team was at Nottingham,
the numerous girls of the lace-factories took the liveliest
interest in each and every member. They wrote letters
to several, and the most daring girls would invent some
pretext for addressing an Australian without the for-
mality of an introduction. One pretty little lace miss
threw herself in the path of the great Giffen, and George’s
theatrical limp had been rather pronounced, so the kind
little girl accosted George with, “Excuse me, Mr. Giffen,
but I merely wanted to inquire if anything serious is the
matter with you?” The Australian all-round cricketer
replied, “ No, little woman ! nothing serious,” and added
by way of facetiousness, “ housemaid’s knee, that’s all! ”
“ Oh, dear! Mr. Giffen ! really! may I ask the house-
maid’s name ? ”
* « * *
SmiterLyons’recent bursts of form are attributed
by many of his fellow cricketers to the steadiness of life
consequent on a happy marriage. Blondin, they say, is a
model husband.
One reason of the recent Australian eleven’s
success was the absence of a skiter. Trott and Gregory’s
splendid stand in the first test-match would have made
swelled head almost excusable in any man, but neither of
the pair ever opened the subject with his fellow mem-
bers of the eleven, or with anyone else. Iredale, too,
after his unprecedented run of big scores, kept modestly
silent. He never referred to his 108 in the second test-
match even, although that innings meant everything to
Iredale. For it gave the lie direct to the many detractors
who were always anxious to assert that the long chap had
little heart. Iredale showed plenty of pluck in England-
taking 36 out of 38 catches in the long field, with all eyes
upon him.
Perhaps the man who showed the most pluck in
England was Donnan. Several of the eleven say, “If
Donnan couldn’t get runs any other way, he got them by
pluck.”
Giffen’s 80 in the second test-match is con-
sidered the best innings this time by an Australian
Eleven man. Iredale, who was the great man’s partner,
says that to watch Giffen compile that 80 was to learn
how to play cricket.
The man who did most work and showed most
activity was “Jonah” Jones. “Rock ’em in” was a
great bowler in England, where the wickets helped him
and brought his lightning ones back with deadly effect.
And when they missed he didn’t swear or cry or sulk
—he just plugged in for a rib or a wicket next over, or, if
fielding, streaked like an emu for the boundary. A strong
goer is Jonah—probably out of the many thousand men
who watched him in England there were not half-a-dozen
who could have come into the field and “ mixed it ” with
him.
The best fieldsman the Australians met in Eng-
land was Mordaunt, of Oxford. He would field a red-hot
ball, knock the wicket down, turn a double somersault,
and dance a break-down, all in the space of 3sec.
In the Australians’ first match with Yorkshire,
Hirst, the county bowler, knocked about the visitors in
the first innings; fast balls from him struck gruelling
blows on batsmen’s arms, legs, bodies. And whenever a
struck player squirmed round, the fair-play crowd of
Yorkshire clodhoppers, wallopers and oafs grunted,
squealed and chuckled in ecstasy of applause. By and
bye, Yorkshire’s first innings began. The crowd saw
Jonah Jones run up to the crease, but it never saw the
ball. It saw a winged batsman fluttering round the
wicket, and it heard the shriek of the strong Englishman
in his agony. And the deadly work went on. New
stumps were brought, new bats, new batsmen, to fill the
gaps of the broken up and slain. The crowd couldn’t
stand it ; the crowd roared with a din that threatened a
riot: “Tak um off ! play fair cricket! don’t kill men!”
To please them Jones was taken off, and the crowd heaved
a sigh of relief, deep and loud as the sigh of the ocean.
But Eady was put on instead of Jones, and Eady’s first
ball, a terrific wind-jammer, struck Hirst himself a
sickenin’ thud. “ Oh-h-h,” bellowed the Yorkshire herd
in agony. “Tak um off, too! That ’s bad as
t’other.”
Three swagmen died on the track within
70 miles of Broken Hill during the awful heat of
Xmas and Boxing days. One was “unknown,”
another was supposed to be somebody, only the
third was really identified. One man died on the
very edge of a tank, another just in sight of what
he had crawled for many hours to reach.
What will they do with it ? Last week,
at Sydney, someone in authority condemned about
15cwt. of tea to be destroyed as unfit for human
consumption. Accordingly, it was emptied out
at Mt. Rennie rubbish-tip, where dozens of men,
women, and children eagerly gathered it up in
bags and tins from among the odorous rubbish
and trekked joyfully citywards.
Although both N.S.W. political parties
ostentatiously disclaim any attempt to introduce
party feeling into the Federal Convention election,
all the same there will be a big under-current
struggling to down Reid, and put Barton and
O’Connor as far up the poll as possible. All the
political enmity Reid has aroused by his policy,
and all the dissatisfaction daily growing against
Freetraders, will be concentrated upon “Jolly and
Fat.”
Writes a Melbourne correspondent :
“ With regard to titles in general and Turner’s
m particular: he ought to have consulted his
party before accepting the bauble, for what-
ever happens to him, the small-fry will have to
suffer in the electorates. That is proved by the
downfall of Bibbs, Stout, Berry, Patterson, and
others with their parties. Their decline began
from the title. In a case of Turner’s kind, he had
probably the choice of consulting his wife or his
political party. He was bound to consult one or
other, if not both, from the result, one gathers
that he consulted his wife. Hence the title. Now
we shall see whether his example will swell the
averages of political downfall through Imperial
branding, or whether he will be the exception
which proves the rule.”

THE ENORMOUS and annually increasing
quantity of OIL-CAKES consumed in the
United Kingdom, Europe, and America has
proved beyond question that a liberal but judi-
cious outlay on the part of Dairy Farmers and

The Strange Story of the
Smuggled JPills.
A representative of the Stoke Newington and
Islington Recorder writes Seldom have I
come across a more remarkable experience than
that related to me a few days since by Mr. John
Cole, of 46 Cressington-road, Stoke, Newington.
Mr. Cole, a well-built man of thirty-seven years
of age, was anxious rather than otherwise that the
facts should be universally known. The manner
in which he told them stamped the tale
With the Impress op Truth.
“ I must tell you,” Mr. Cole began, “ that the
smuggling of which you have heard was not an
ordinary case, but was occasioned by the fact that
I was ill. My illness was both mental and physi-
cal. I had been in one situation for about 20
years, and, leaving, I found it more difficult than
I had imagined to obtain other employment.
Constant disappointments depressed me to a. very
considerable extent. I could not sleep at nights,
my appetite failed, and I fell into a low, despon-
dent state. I wanted to be left alone, and
would walk for miles aimlessly about. Indeed,
my whole nervous system seemed completely
shattered, and my friends thought that I had be-
come very strange in my manners. I had become
so weak that I could scarcely stand, and began to
have terrible pains in my head. I became an out-
patient at an infirmary, but one day I was taken
to a Lunatic Asylum.”
Mr. Cole did not like the asylum, continues the
reporter. The pains in his head became worse, he
could not sleep, and frequently sat up all night
holding his head between his hands.
“ After I had suffered this misery for about
three months,” continued Mr. Cole,
“ my Wife Smuggled
in to me a box of pills. Mrs. Cole had been told
by a neighbor of the wonderful efficacy of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and was
anxious to do anything she could to relieve me of
the terrible pains from which I suffered.
“I was very dubious,” Mr. Cole frankly ad-
mitted, “ but certainly found relief from the first
box. I began to sleep, the weak and trembling
state of my limbs was to a great extent diminished,
and the pains in the head were not so frequent.
When I had taken the third box I was so much
improved in health that 1 asked to be set to work
about the wards, and
Daily I Grew Stronger
under the benign influence of Dr. Williams’ Pills.
I continued to take an occasional dose, and in six
weeks I was as well as lam to day. My health
and mental condition were such that the medical
officer saw no further ground for my detention,
and I once more became a free man.”
It is not only in cases like those described above,
however, that Dr. Williams’ Pills are so valuable.
They have cured paralysis, locomotor ata,xy, rheu-
matism, and sciatica ; also all diseases arising from
impoverishment of the blood, scrofula, rickets,
chronic erysipelas, consumption of the bowels and
lungs, anaemia, pale and sallow complexion,
general muscular weakness, loss of appetite, pal-
pitations, pains in the back, nervous headache,
early decay, all forms of female weakness, and
hysteria. These Pills are a tonic, not a purgative.
They are genuine only with the full name, Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and are sold
by chemists and storekeepers generally, or the Dr.
Williams Medicine Coy., Queen’s Place, Sydney,
will forward on receipt of stamps or post order one
box for 2s 9d, or half-dozen for 14s 9d.*

THIS Company is being formed to acquire the well-known Melbourne United Mines, at 1.0. U.
(Bulong) held under leases No. E 504 (now 36 Y) and 330 E (now 11 Y), and known respectively
as the Light of Europe and the Arbroth, aggregating 18 acres. The Queen Margaret lode has been
proved in the Melbourne IJmted by developments, almost identical with those in the Queen Margaret, as
will be seen by the accompanying plan.
An underlay shaft is down 175 ft., and
is connected with No. 2 underlay shaft
by a drive of 193 ft. long at the 90ft.
levels. The North shaft is down 50ft.,
and altogether there is 410 ft. of driv-
ing done, and about 130 ft. of cross-
cutting, proving the lode in the
dimensions stated. The Eastern lode
is proved at about the same width as
the Queen Margaret, i.e., 30ft. On
the footwall side the stone is of only
a few pennyweight grade, but towards
the hanging wall the rich stringers of
quartz and iron become wider and
more numerous in their occurrence,
making the lode a payable one through-
out.
Arrangements can be made for the
lease of 10 head of Stampers from the
Bulong Mining Tramway and Ore Re-
duction Company Limited, whose plant
is in course of erection and will
660 ft.
shortly be completed. The contract
with the Mining- Tramway and Ore Reduction Company will include the free transport
of Ore from the mine to the Battery on the railway now being laid for the Company, which
will also crush for the White Horse Company, whose Mine occurs between the Melbourne United and
the Quefe been reported on by Mr. Simpson, Manager of the Queen Margaret Mine, and Mr.
T. Hewitson, Manager of the Ivanhoe Mine, and in their opinion the mine is an excellent property.
Following are the results of eight tests of Ore from the various levels made by Professor H.
Rowley, of St. George’s Terrace, Perth.

Report of Capt. SIMPSON,
MANAGER OF THE QUEEN MARGARET MINE.
Received by Wire, January 6th, 1897.
To Randolph Bedford, Melbourne, —
Have carefully inspected your property situated at Bulong, known as Melbourne United, and beg
to report thereon as follows Your property consists of two blocks, namely six and twelve acres, and
is situated due north of, and adjoining the White Horse Mine, the latter adjoining the Queen Margaret
on the north. A costeen has been sunk on a nice looking ironstone outcrop, from which very rich gold
has recently been taken, and samples forwarded to Melbourne. On block number five hundred and
four, which is the south block, and consists of twelve acres, two shafts have been sunk. Number one
shaft is on the underlie, and is down to depth of one hundred and seventy-seven feet. Drives north
and south, and crosscuts east and west, have been driven from each level, opening up large areas of
payable ore, bearing precisely the same characteristics as on the Queen Margaret. Number three shaft is
situated five hundred feet north of number one. It is down to a depth of fifty feet, and from it drives,
north forty feet, and south one hundred feet, have been put in—the last twenty feet have been driven
in lode formation. On block number three-thirty, consisting of six acres, number two shaft has been
sunk to a depth of ninety feet. A drive south has been put in eighty-five feet. A crosscut east from
the end of this drive has been driven thirty-five feet across the lode formation. High grade samples
were taken from the various levels and crosscuts and gave highly satisfactory results, the average being
three-and-a-half ozs. to the ton. About a thousand feet in sinking, driving, and crosscutting has been
done. There is a plentiful supply of timber surrounding the property, and water can be obtained at a
depth of two hundred feet. In conclusion I have pleasure in stating that from the excellent position of
your property and the fact that the Queen Margaret lode traverses its entire length, and that you have
large bodies of payable ore already opened up, 1 have no hesitation in stating that given systematic
and careful management, the mine will, in the near future, be classed amongst the dividend paying
mines of this colony.
F. SIMPSON,
Bulong, 1.0. U., W.A. Manager, Queen Margaret Mine.
Report by Capt. T. HEWITSON,
MANAGER INYANHOE MINE.
To Randolph Bedford, Melbourne. Received by wire, 9th January, 1897.
Have examined your property, The Melbourne United Mine, at Bulong, consisting of two leases of
twelve and six acres respectively, and situated adjoining the White Horse and Queen Margaret Mines.
The surface contour shows a gold-bearing outcrop intermixed with ironstone, traversing the greater
part of the three properties ; lode characteristics being very similar to Queen Margaret, and in places
is 35 feet thick. It is a contact lode interlaced with quartz veins running into the hanging wall of lode.
These intersections frequently show very rich ore. Trials from the kaolin, a short distance from the
veins, show the ore to be of poorer grade. A good deal of development work has been done. Three
shafts are sunk, one is fifty feet deep, another is ninety feet, and the third is one hundred and seventy-
five feet deep.
Four levels have been opened up at fifty feet, ninety feet, one hundred and fifty feet and one
hundred and seventy feet deep respectively. I have taken a great number of samples, showing from a
trace to seven ounces per ton, and the average is about two-and-a-half ounces. The property is well situ-
ated for mine timber and water, and with good machinery and judicious management I deem it
promising property.
T. HEWITSON, Ivanhoe Gold Mining Co.

Dear Bulletin, — “With a groan, he
sprang to his feet, and dropped his pen as if it were
a serpent.” That quote is from a book of stories by
Lilian Quiller Couch, a near relative of “Q.,”
who ought to have known better. A considerable
experience in handling snakes has convinced me
that about the last thing to do with a serpent is
to drop it. “ Hold on for all you’re worth ! ” is
the better advice. I speak as one having authority,
for I have had the pleasure of gripping and box-
ing, among others, an escaped female copperhead
at full term of pregnancy, and the temper of that
kind of wriggler is indescribable. If you happen
to hold a snake, hold it hard and hold it tight—
especially if you are within four walls. If you
have it by the neck, it can’t slew round to bite
your hand. If you have it about the middle, the
chances are about a million to one that it bites I
through a coat-sleeve or shirt-sleeve, which will j
absorb at least two-thirds of the poison, and that |
you will only be a bit frightened and inclined to
be sick. If you have it by the tail somewhere, even
that is better in some cases than not having it at
all. Loose snakes shoot for dark holes, and
trouser-legs attract them. That Family Herald
metaphor of dropping anything “ as if it were a
serpent ” has got to go. With all venomous
snakes, and venomous snakes are comparatively
small, the proper text is : “ Hold hard and hold
tight.” But with pythons, boas, and the big
Queensland non-venomous snakes (say all snakes
over 5ft.), get out and get quickly. One man
I know had such a snake by head and tail. He
wasn’t a bit afraid of it, but when it, in its strug-
gles, got its body looped round his neck, he got a
very fair exhibition of what slow strangulation
feels like.—Yours, J.S.R.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
Writes a Thursday Island (Q.) correspon-
dent : —The extinction of the Australian by the
Chow and the Jap. is getting on remarkably well
in these parts, and the heathen in his blindness
grows self-assertive. Some days back two white
men from the south obtained a permit from the
Divisional Board to run a humble show—a steam
riding-gallery—on a piece of land fronting “our
harbor.” They opened to fair business, and de-
cided to remain till the divers and the crews of
the pearl-fleet came in. Then it occurred to the
Jap. and Chow storekeepers, likewise the fan-tan
sharks and the pak-ah-poo men and the Asiatic
brothel-keepers, that this little show might take
some of the money that would otherwise come
their way, and they presented an indignant peti-
tion demanding its removal and the summary
ejection of the two showmen. One publican, the
local garbage-man, and sundry white loafers
backed up the petition. So it has come to this, that
the nigger now demands the chucking of the Aus-
tralian out of his own country because he is in
the nigger’s road. The petition was rejected, and
the wildly-astonished heathen withdrew to con-
sider the matter ; the idea of a white man not
being thrown out when the Jap. and Chow
interest of Thursday Island demanded it was so
novel that it struck the idolator all of a heap, and
he couldn’t assimilate the fact all at once. But
things are only in their early stages ; when they
fully realise the situation the Chow and Jap. will
elect each other to the Divisional Board, and do
their own chucking out, and by-and-bye a Mongol
policeman will arrest the white man for sneezing
in the presence of Joss. The good time is coming.

AN INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST
